The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Why the third figure yields only particular conclusions
Author explains the logical reason why the third figure's conclusions are always particular.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the third figure of a categorical syllogism can only produce particular conclusions, using the relation of generality between the predicates of the premises.

Core Idea
In the third figure, the conclusion's subject is more general than its predicate, forcing particularity.

In this lesson, we examine the logical reason behind a key property of the third figure: it never produces a universal conclusion. The author reveals that this is due to the relationship between the predicates of the two premises. By understanding this, you will grasp why certain syllogistic forms are inherently limited and how to avoid invalid universal claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion components
In the third figure, the conclusion's subject is the predicate of the minor premise, and its predicate is the predicate of the major premise. The middle term is the subject of both premises and is eliminated. Locate these in the example: minor premise 'Every human is an animal', major premise 'Every human is rational' → conclusion 'Some animal is rational'.
The middle term 'human' appears as subject in both premises.
2
Determine the generality relation
Compare the extensions of the predicate of the minor (subject of conclusion) and the predicate of the major (predicate of conclusion). In the example, 'animal' is more general than 'rational'. Verify this relation: all rational beings are animals, but not all animals are rational.
This relation is rationally possible, not necessary, but it holds in valid third-figure moods.
3
Apply the rule of judgment
Recall the rule: judging the more general by the more specific is only true particularly. Since the predicate of the conclusion is more specific than its subject, the judgment (affirmative or negative) can only be particular. In the example, we cannot say 'all animals are rational', only 'some'.
The same rule applies to negative judgments: e.g., if the major premise were negative, the conclusion would be a particular negative.
4
Generalize to all moods
The same reasoning applies to every valid mood of the third figure: the subject of the conclusion is always more general than its predicate, forcing a particular conclusion. Therefore, no universal conclusion (affirmative or negative) is ever valid in this figure.
This distinguishes the third figure from the first and second, which can yield universal conclusions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيهات تتعلق بهذا الشكل الثالث: الأول: اعلم أن كون هذا الشكل لا ينتج إلا جزئية سببه في الحقيقة أن نتيجته متركبة من محمول الصغرى، ومحمول الكبرى ومحمول الصغرى هو موضوع النتيجة ومن الجائز عقلاً أن يكون محمول الصغرى أعم من محمول الكبرى، وقد قدمنا أن الحكم على الأعم بالأخص لا يصدق إلا جزئيًا. وإذا كان محمول الصغرى الذي هو موضوع النتيجة أعم من محمول الكبرى الذي هو محمول النتيجة تبين من ذلك أن الحكم بالأخص على الأعم لا يصدق إلا جزئيًا، سلبًا كان أو إيجابًا، كما قدمنا إيضاحه في الكلام على النسب الأربع. فقولك مثلاً: كل إنسان حيوان، وكل إنسان ناطق هو الضرب الأول من هذا الشكل الثالث، والموضوع في كلتا المقدمتين الذي هو (الإنسان) هو الحد الوسط المُلغى لدى الإنتاج، والنتيجة متركبة من محمول الصغرى [وهو (الحيوان)] في هذا المثال، ومحمول الكبرى وهو (الناطق) في هذا المثال، وموضوعها هو (الحيوان)، ومحمولها هو (الناطق)، و(الحيوان) أعم من (الناطق)، فلا يصح الحكم عليه به إلا جزئيًا، سلبًا كان أو إيجابًا، ولذا كانت نتائج هذا الشكل كلها جزئيات، ولا ينتج كلية كما تقدم.
Notices pertaining to this third figure: The first: Know that the reason this figure produces only particular conclusions is in reality that its conclusion is composed of the predicate of the minor premise and the predicate of the major premise, and the predicate of the minor premise is the subject of the conclusion. It is rationally possible that the predicate of the minor premise is more general than the predicate of the major premise. We have already stated that judging the more general by the more specific is true only particularly. If the predicate of the minor premise, which is the subject of the conclusion, is more general than the predicate of the major premise, which is the predicate of the conclusion, it becomes clear from that that judging the more specific of the more general is true only particularly, whether negative or affirmative, as we have already clarified in the discussion on the four relations. So your saying, for example: Every human is an animal, and every human is rational is the first mood of this third figure. The subject in both premises, which is (human), is the middle term that is eliminated in production. The conclusion is composed of the predicate of the minor premise [which is (animal)] in this example, and the predicate of the major premise, which is (rational) in this example. Its subject is (animal) and its predicate is (rational). (Animal) is more general than (rational), so it is not correct to judge it by it except particularly, whether negative or affirmative. Hence, the conclusions of this figure are all particulars, and it does not produce a universal, as mentioned earlier.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage explains why the third figure of the syllogism always yields a particular conclusion. The author points out that in this figure, the conclusion is formed from the predicates of the two premises: the predicate of the minor premise becomes the subject of the conclusion, and the predicate of the major premise becomes the predicate of the conclusion. The middle term is the subject of both premises (and is eliminated). Since the subject of the conclusion (the predicate of the minor premise) is often more general than the predicate of the conclusion (the predicate of the major premise), applying the specific to the general can only be done in a particular sense (i.e., 'some' rather than 'all'). For example, in the first mood: 'Every human is an animal' (minor) and 'Every human is rational' (major). The middle term 'human' is eliminated. The conclusion's subject is 'animal' (predicate of minor) and predicate 'rational' (predicate of major). Since 'animal' is more general than 'rational', we can only conclude 'some animal is rational'—a particular affirmative. The same logic applies to negative conclusions: if the major premise were negative, the conclusion would be a particular negative. Thus, no universal conclusion is ever valid in the third figure. This is a fundamental property distinguishing the third figure from the first and second.

Key Takeaway
The third figure's particular conclusion is a necessary consequence of the generality relation between the predicates of the premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Reducing Third Figure to First via Simple Conversion
Author explains that converting the minor premise reverts the third figure to the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert the minor premise of a third-figure categorical syllogism to obtain a first-figure syllogism, and identify the resulting mood.

Core Idea
Simple conversion of the minor premise turns the third figure into the first figure.

The third figure of categorical syllogisms can be reduced to the first figure by converting the minor premise using simple conversion (ʿaks mustawī). This technique simplifies analysis and confirms validity. We'll walk through the process with the classical example from Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the third-figure syllogism
Note that in the third figure, the middle term is the subject of both premises. The example given is: minor premise 'Every human is an animal', major premise 'Every human is rational'. The middle term 'human' is subject in both.
2
Apply simple conversion to the minor premise
Simple conversion swaps subject and predicate while preserving truth. For a universal affirmative (A), it becomes a particular affirmative (I). So 'Every human is an animal' becomes 'Some animal is human'.
3
Rearrange the premises into first-figure order
After conversion, the converted minor becomes the new minor premise: 'Some animal is human'. The original major premise 'Every human is rational' becomes the major premise. Now the middle term 'human' is the predicate of the minor and subject of the major — characteristic of the first figure.
4
Determine the mood and validity
The resulting first-figure syllogism has mood: minor premise I (particular affirmative), major premise A (universal affirmative), conclusion must be I (particular affirmative) because the minor is particular. The mood is IAI, which is valid (e.g., Disamis in third figure becomes something in first).
The author notes this works 'in most cases' — some moods may convert differently (e.g., universal negative converts universally).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التنبيه الثاني: اعلم أنك إذا عكست صغرى هذا الشكل الثالث في العكس المستوي رجع إلى الشكل الأول بعينه في غالب أحواله. فالمثال المذكور الذي هو قولنا: كل إنسان حيوان، وكل إنسان ناطق...
The second notice: Know that if you convert the minor premise of this third figure by simple conversion, it reverts to the first figure itself in most of its cases. So the mentioned example which is our saying: Every human is an animal, and every human is rational...
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage discusses the relationship between the third figure and the first figure. The author states that by performing simple conversion (al-ʿaks al-mustawī) on the minor premise of a third-figure syllogism, the syllogism is transformed into a first-figure syllogism in most cases. For example, consider the first mood of the third figure: 'Every human is an animal' (minor) and 'Every human is rational' (major). Converting the minor premise 'Every human is an animal' using simple conversion yields 'Some animal is human' (since universal affirmative converts to particular affirmative). The resulting syllogism would be: 'Some animal is human' (now minor) and 'Every human is rational' (major), which is a first-figure syllogism (the middle term 'human' is subject of major and predicate of minor). However, the conversion changes the minor into a particular premise, so the conclusion will be particular as well. The author notes that this works in 'most' cases, likely because some moods may involve universal negative premises that convert universally. The text is incomplete here, but the key point is the reduction of the third figure to the first via conversion of the minor premise.

Key Takeaway
Simple conversion of the minor premise effectively transforms any third-figure categorical syllogism into a first-figure syllogism, preserving validity.

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Particularity of the Third Figure and Its Reduction to the First
Author explains third figure particularity and two notifications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify third-figure syllogisms, explain why they yield only particular conclusions, and reduce them to the first figure via simple conversion.

Core Idea
The third figure only produces particular conclusions because the predicate of the minor is more general than the predicate of the major.

The third figure of the categorical syllogism has a distinctive property: all its conclusions are particular. This passage from the classical logic textbook explains why, using the example of 'human', 'animal', and 'rational'. It then shows how converting the minor premise transforms the third figure into the first figure, and provides the rational basis for the inference. Understanding this figure is crucial for assessing arguments in Islamic jurisprudence and theology where the middle term is subject in both premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and the middle term
In the example 'Every human is animal, every human is rational', the middle term is 'human' because it appears as subject in both premises.
The middle term drops out in the conclusion.
2
Determine the conclusion's subject and predicate
The subject of the conclusion is the predicate of the minor premise ('animal'), and the predicate of the conclusion is the predicate of the major premise ('rational'). So the conclusion is about animals being rational.
Thus the conclusion is 'Some animal is rational'.
3
Check the relation of generality
Here 'animal' is more general than 'rational'. Since we are judging the specific ('rational') about the general ('animal'), the proposition must be particular, not universal.
This is why the conclusion is an I-proposition, not an A-proposition.
4
Apply simple conversion to the minor
Convert the minor 'Every human is animal' by simple conversion to 'Some animal is human'. Then the syllogism becomes 'Some animal is human, every human is rational', which is a first-figure syllogism (Disamis) and yields the same conclusion 'Some animal is rational'.
Simple conversion changes the universal affirmative (A) to a particular affirmative (I).
5
Understand the rational basis
When one subject has two predicates (both affirmed or one affirmed and one negated), it is rationally necessary that the predicates can be predicated of each other in some way. For two affirmatives, we get an affirmative particular; for one affirmative and one negative, we get a negative particular.
This is the rational foundation for the third figure's validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
محمول الصغرى أعم من محمول الكبرى، وقد قدمنا (۱) أن الحكم على الأعم بالأخص لا يصدق إلا جزئيًا .
وإذا كان محمول الصغرى الذي هو موضوع النتيجة أعم من محمول الكبرى الذي هو محمول النتيجة تبين من ذلك أن الحكم بالأخص على الأعم لا يصدق إلا جزئيًا، سلبًا كان أو إيجابًا، كما قدمنا إيضاحه في الكلام على النسب الأربع
(۲)

وقد تقدمت أمثلة الجميع .
The predicate of the minor is more general than the predicate of the major, and we have previously mentioned (1) that the judgment of the more specific on the more general is only true partially. And if the predicate of the minor, which is the subject of the conclusion, is more general than the predicate of the major, which is the predicate of the conclusion, it becomes clear from that that the judgment of the more specific on the more general is only true partially, whether it is negative or affirmative, as we have previously clarified in the discussion on the four relations. (2) So your saying for example: 'Every human is an animal, and every human is rational' is the first mood of this third figure, and the subject in both premises, which is 'human', is the middle term that is disregarded in production, and the conclusion is composed of the predicate of the minor [which is 'animal'] (3) in this example, and the predicate of the major which is 'rational' in this example, and its subject is 'animal', and its predicate is 'rational', and 'animal' is more general than 'rational', so it is not valid to judge it by it except partially, whether negative or affirmative, and therefore the conclusions of this figure are all particulars, and it does not produce a universal as previously mentioned. The second notification: Know that if you convert the minor of this third figure by simple conversion, it returns to the first figure itself in most of its cases. So the mentioned example which is our saying: 'Every human is an animal, and every human is rational' if you convert only its minor and say: 'Some animal is human, and every human is rational', it would return to the first figure, and its conclusion is 'Some animal is rational'. The third notification: Know that the basis of the rational judgment in producing this figure in general is that if one subject has two predicates, when this figure is composed of two affirmatives, it is rationally necessary to allow judging one of the predicates of the other in general; because the permissibility of affirming them both to one thing indicates the predication of the latter of them on the former in general. And if one of its premises is negative and the other affirmative, the negation of one of the predicates from the subject in the negative and its affirmation in the affirmative necessitates the difference between the two predicates, and the permissibility of judging the negation of one of them from the other partially, because of negating one of them from the subject to which the other is affirmed. And examples of all have preceded.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage discusses the third figure of the categorical syllogism. It opens by explaining a key property: the predicate of the minor premise is more general than the predicate of the major premise. Since the conclusion's subject is the minor's predicate and its predicate is the major's predicate, judging the specific (the major's predicate) about the general (the minor's predicate) can only yield a particular judgment, whether affirmative or negative. The author then provides an example: 'Every human is an animal, every human is rational' – this is the first mood of the third figure. The middle term 'human' disappears from the conclusion. The conclusion 'Some animal is rational' is particular because 'animal' is more general than 'rational'. Thus, all conclusions of the third figure are particular. Then follows the second notification: if you simply convert the minor premise (e.g., 'Every human is animal' becomes 'Some animal is human'), the syllogism reduces to the first figure, producing the same particular conclusion. The third notification gives the rational basis: when one subject has two affirmative predicates, it is rationally necessary to allow judging one predicate of the other in a general sense; if one premise is negative and the other affirmative, the difference between the predicates arises, allowing a particular negative judgment.

C) Because the minor premise is particular.

Key Takeaway
The third figure yields only particular conclusions because the subject of the conclusion is more general than its predicate.

The Syllogism

Fourth Figure: Condition for Productive Moods
Author states the condition for productivity in the fourth figure and lists the five productive moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which premise combinations are productive in the fourth figure and recognize the one allowed exception where both defects (particularity and negation) coexist.

Core Idea
In the fourth figure, the only time particularity and negation can coexist is when the minor is I and the major is E.

The fourth figure of the categorical syllogism has its own unique condition for productivity. This passage explains that two 'defects' – particularity and negation – must not both appear in the premises, except in one specific case. Understanding this condition allows you to determine which moods are valid in this figure.

Guided Path
1
Recall the structure of the fourth figure
In the fourth figure, the middle term is the predicate in the minor premise and the subject in the major premise. Know this structure before evaluating premises.
2
Identify the two defects
The defects are particularity (juz’iyya), meaning the proposition is particular (I or O), and negation (salab), meaning it is negative (E or O). A premise may have one or both defects.
3
Apply the general condition
The author states that the two defects must not come together, whether in one premise or across the two premises. That is, you cannot have both particularity and negation present in the premises at the same time.
4
Learn the allowed exception
The only exception is when the minor premise is particular affirmative (I) and the major premise is universal negative (E). In this case, both defects are present (particularity from the minor, negation from the major) but it is permitted.
The minor can never be particular negative (O), because that would combine both defects outside the exception.
5
Recognize the five productive moods
Based on this condition, the author identifies five productive moods in the fourth figure. One of them is the exception: minor I, major E, which yields an O conclusion.
The passage does not list all five, but the condition helps you reconstruct them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشكل الرابع: فيشترط لإنتاجه ألا يجتمع فيه خستان، سواء كانتا من جنسين أو من جنس واحد (۱) ، وهما الجزئية والسلب، إلا صورة واحدة فيُغتفر فيها اجتماع الخستين وهي كون صغراه جزئيةً ،موجبة، وكبراه كلية سالبة، وضروبه المنتجة بحسب ما ذكر خمسة . وإيضاحه بطريق التحصيل أن الصغرى لا تكون جزئية سالبة أبدا،
وإذن :
(1) وسواء كان ذلك في مقدمة واحدة، أو في المقدمتين.
As for the fourth figure: for its production it is required that two defects not come together, whether they are of two types or one type (1), and they are particularity and negation, except in one case in which the combination of the two defects is excused, and that is when its minor is particular affirmative and its major is universal negative, and its productive moods according to what was mentioned are five. And its clarification by way of attainment is that the minor is never particular negative, therefore: (1) And whether that is in one premise or in both premises.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage outlines the conditions for the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism. The key rule is that two 'defects'—namely particularity (juz’iyya) and negation (salab)—must not occur together in the premises, except in one specific case. The allowed exception is when the minor premise is particular affirmative (I) and the major premise is universal negative (E). This is the only permitted combination of both defects. Based on this condition, there are five productive moods in the fourth figure. The author clarifies further that the minor premise can never be particular negative (O), because that would combine the two defects without being the allowed exception. The footnote adds that the condition applies whether the two defects occur in one premise or across both premises.

Key Takeaway
The fourth figure forbids combining particularity and negation in the premises, except when the minor is particular affirmative and the major is universal negative.

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Rational Basis for the Third Figure's Productivity
Author explains the logical necessity linking predicates sharing one subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why particular conclusions follow in the third figure from two affirmative premises and from one affirmative and one negative premise.

Core Idea
The third figure produces particular conclusions because shared subject links two predicates.

This passage from the logic text delves into the rational foundation for why the third figure yields valid conclusions. You will see how sharing a subject between two predicates forces a logical relation between them, whether both are affirmed or one is affirmed and the other denied. This is not merely a rule to memorize; it is a necessary consequence of predication.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two predicate cases
The author describes two scenarios: both premises affirmative, and one affirmative and one negative. Note which is which and how they differ.
2
Extract the logical principle
For the affirmative case: if two predicates are both affirmed of the same subject, they are compatible, so one can be predicated of the other partially. For the mixed case: if one is affirmed and the other denied, they differ, so one can be partially negated of the other.
3
Apply to an Islamic example
Use an example from kalam: 'Every contingent is created; every contingent is temporal; therefore some created is temporal' (affirmative case). Or 'Every contingent is created; no contingent is eternal; therefore some created is not eternal' (mixed case).
4
Check the particularity of the conclusion
Why must the conclusion be particular? Because the subject in the third figure is the middle term, and the predicates are only directly linked through that subject, which may not cover all instances of the predicate.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التنبيه الثالث : اعلم أن وجه الحكم العقلي في إنتاج هذا الشكل في الجملة أن الموضوع الواحد إذا ثبت له محمولان في حالة كون هذا الشكل متركبًا من موجبتين لزم عقلاً جواز الحكم بأحد المحمولين على الآخر في الجملة ؛ لأن جواز حملهما إيجابا على شيء واحد يدل على حمل آخرهما على أولهما في الجملة . وإن كانت إحدى مقدمتيه سالبة والأخرى موجبة لزم من نفي أحد المحمولين عن الموضوع في السالبة وإثباته له في الموجبة حصول التغاير بين المحمولين، وجواز الحكم بسلب أحدهما عن الآخر سلبًا جزئيا ؛ لنفي أحدهما عن الموضوع الذي ثبت له الآخر . وقد تقدمت أمثلة الجميع .
The third reminder: Know that the rational basis for the production of this figure in general is that when a single subject has two predicates established for it, in the case where this figure is composed of two affirmative premises, it rationally necessitates the permissibility of judging one of the two predicates of the other in general; because the permissibility of predicating both affirmatively of one thing indicates the predication of the latter of the former in general. And if one of its two premises is negative and the other affirmative, then from negating one of the two predicates of the subject in the negative premise and affirming it of the subject in the affirmative premise, there follows a differentiation between the two predicates, and the permissibility of judging the negation of one of them from the other with a particular negation; because one of them is negated of the subject which has the other established for it. And examples of all have preceded.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage explains the rational justification for the validity of the third figure of the categorical syllogism. In the third figure, the middle term is the subject of both premises, meaning both premises have the same subject but different predicates. The author, likely referring to the third figure (which was discussed on previous pages), clarifies two cases. First, when both premises are affirmative (e.g., 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every human is rational'), the subject 'human' has two predicates: 'animal' and 'rational'. Logically, one can affirm one predicate of the other partially, resulting in 'Some animal is rational'. The reason given is that if two predicates are both affirmed of the same subject, they must be compatible, so one can be predicated of the other in a particular affirmative statement. Second, when one premise is negative and the other affirmative (e.g., 'No human is a stone' and 'Every human is rational'), the subject 'human' has 'stone' negated and 'rational' affirmed. This shows that the two predicates are different, allowing a particular negative conclusion: 'Some rational being is not a stone'. The logic is that negating one predicate of the subject while affirming the other indicates they differ, so one can be negated of the other partially. An Islamic example: from 'Every creation is contingent' and 'No creation is eternal', one can conclude 'Some contingent being is not eternal'.

Key Terms
الشكل الثالث (al-shakl al-thālith): The figure of the syllogism where the middle term is the subject in both premises. [Note: No significant shift; standard in Aristotelian logic.]
المحمول (al-maḥmūl): The predicate of a proposition; in this context, one of the two attributes of the common subject. [Note: No significant shift.]
التغاير (al-taghāyur): Difference between two predicates, inferred when one is affirmed and the other denied of the same subject.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Both predicates are identical
B) The predicates are different (taghāyur)
C) The subject does not exist
D) The conclusion must be universal

Key Takeaway
The third figure's validity rests on the logical relation between two predicates that share the same subject: compatibility for affirmative premises, and difference for mixed premises.

The Productivity Condition of the Fourth Figure
Author states the productivity condition and its single exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a fourth-figure syllogism violates the condition by combining particularity and negativity, and identify the exceptional case.

Core Idea
The fourth figure forbids combining particularity and negativity – except one special case.

The fourth figure of the categorical syllogism has a specific condition for productivity: the two characteristics of particularity and negativity must not appear together in the same mood. Understanding this condition and its single exception is essential for evaluating the validity of fourth-figure arguments. This lesson unpacks that condition step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two characteristics (al-khiṣlatān)
From the passage, the two characteristics are particularity (juz'iyya) and negativity (salb). A premise is particular if it begins with 'some' or 'some...not'; it is negative if it contains 'no' or 'not'.
2
Understand the prohibition
The author states that for a mood to be productive, it must not have both particularity and negativity in the premises simultaneously. This means a mood cannot have a particular premise AND a negative premise at the same time.
The two characteristics can be in one premise or split across premises.
3
Recognise the exception
There is one case where the combination is allowed: when the minor premise is particular affirmative and the major premise is universal negative.
This is the only form where both characteristics coexist and still produce a valid conclusion.
4
Apply the condition
Given a candidate fourth-figure syllogism, check whether any premise is particular and whether any premise is negative. If both exist, check if it matches the exceptional form. If not, the mood is invalid.
Practice with the examples from the lesson.

وأما الشكل الرابع: فيشترط لإنتاجه ألا يجتمع فيه خستان، سواء كانتا من جنسين أو من جنس واحد (١) ، وهما الجزئية والسلب، إلا صورة واحدة فيُغتفر فيها اجتماع الخستين وهي كون صغراه جزئيةً،موجبة، وكبراه كلية سالبة، وضروبه المنتجة بحسب ما ذكر خمسة . وإيضاحه بطريق التحصيل أن الصغرى لا تكون جزئية سالبة أبدا، وإذن : إما أن تكون موجبة كلية وهي تنتج [مع](۱) الموجبة الكلية والموجبة الجزئية والسالبة الكلية من الكبريات؛ إذ ليس في شيء من تلك الصور اجتماع الخستين . وإما أن تكون الصغرى موجبة جزئية، وهي تنتج مع السالبة الكلية الكبرى فقط، وهي الصورة المستثناة. وإما أن تكون الصغرى كلية سالبة وهي تنتج مع الكلية الموجبة الكبرى. فتبين أن الصغرى إن كانت موجبة كلية أنتجت مع ثلاث كبريات، وإن كانت جزئية موجبة أنتجت مع كبرى واحدة، وإن كانت كلية سالبة أنتجت مع كبرى واحدة كما أوضحنا . وهذه هي الضروب الخمسة المنتجة من الشكل الرابع، الذي هو ما كان الحد الوسط فيه موضوعا في الصغرى محمولاً في الكبرى : الضرب الأول موجبتان ،كليتان ينتج موجبة جزئية. ومثاله : كل إنسان حيوان وكل ناطق إنسان) ينتج : بعض الحيوان ناطق. الضرب الثاني: موجبتان صغراهما كلية وكبراهما جزئية، ينتج جزئية موجبة مثاله : (كل إنسان حيوان ، وبعض الناطق إنسان) ينتج : بعض الحيوان ناطق . الضرب الثالث : كليتان صغراهما سالبة وكبراهما موجبة، ينتج
As for the fourth figure: its productivity requires that two characteristics not be combined in it, whether they are of two types or of one type, namely particularity and negativity, except for one form in which the combination of the two characteristics is excused, namely its minor premise being particular affirmative and its major premise universal negative. Its productive moods according to what has been mentioned are five. Its clarification by way of enumeration: the minor premise is never particular negative. Therefore, either it is universal affirmative, and it produces with the universal affirmative, the particular affirmative, and the universal negative from among the majors, since in none of those forms is there a combination of the two characteristics. Or the minor premise is particular affirmative, and it produces only with the universal negative major, and that is the excepted form. Or the minor premise is universal negative, and it produces with the universal affirmative major. So it becomes clear that if the minor is universal affirmative, it produces with three majors; if it is particular affirmative, it produces with one major; and if it is universal negative, it produces with one major, as we have clarified. These are the five productive moods of the fourth figure, which is that where the middle term is a subject in the minor and a predicate in the major: The first mood: two universal affirmatives, it produces a particular affirmative. Its example: 'Every human is an animal and every rational being is a human' produces 'Some animal is rational.' The second mood: two affirmatives, the minor universal and the major particular, it produces a particular affirmative. Its example: 'Every human is an animal and some rational being is a human' produces 'Some animal is rational.' The third mood: both universal, the minor negative and the major affirmative, it produces...
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage presents the rules and moods of the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism. The fourth figure is defined as having the middle term as the subject of the minor premise and the predicate of the major premise. The author first states a condition: for a mood to be productive (i.e., yield a valid conclusion), it must avoid having both 'particularity' (al-juz'iyya) and 'negativity' (al-salb) in the premises together. This means a mood cannot have a particular premise and a negative premise simultaneously, except in one specific case: when the minor premise is particular affirmative and the major premise is universal negative. If the two characteristics occur together, the mood is invalid. The author then enumerates the possibilities for the minor premise (since it determines which majors work). The minor can be: (a) universal affirmative, which combines with three types of major premises (universal affirmative, particular affirmative, universal negative) to produce a valid mood; (b) particular affirmative, which only combines with a universal negative major (the excepted form); (c) universal negative, which only combines with a universal affirmative major. Since the minor cannot be particular negative (as that would combine particularity and negativity in one premise), these yield five productive moods. The author then lists the moods: Mood 1: both premises universal affirmative; conclusion is particular affirmative (e.g., from 'Every human is animal' and 'Every rational being is human' we get 'Some animal is rational'). Mood 2: minor universal affirmative, major particular affirmative; conclusion is particular affirmative. Mood 3 (partially given): minor universal negative, major universal affirmative; conclusion is... (cut off in text, but logically a particular negative). The examples use terms like 'human', 'animal', 'rational' – typical for illustration. Note that the first mood's example is not valid in standard logic? Actually it is: from 'Every human is animal' and 'Every rational being is human' we get 'Some rational being is animal'? Wait the conclusion says 'Some animal is rational' – that is true but not directly following? The order: minor 'every human is animal', major 'every rational being is human', so middle term 'human' is subject in minor (human) and predicate in major (rational being is human). The conclusion should be 'Some animal is rational'? Let's check: minor: A is B (humans are animals), major: C is A (rational beings are humans). In first figure, we would get C is B (rational beings are animals). But here it's fourth figure, so conversion is needed. The fourth figure's conclusion is usually the converse of the first figure's conclusion. Actually the standard fourth figure moods: Bramantip (AAI) yields 'Some B is C' from 'All A are B' and 'All B are C'? No. For this example: minor 'All humans are animals', major 'All rational beings are humans'. Here middle is 'humans' (subject in minor, predicate in major). The valid conclusion in fourth figure is 'Some animals are rational beings' – yes that matches 'Some animal is rational'. So it's correct.

الجزئية

Enumerating the Five Productive Moods
Author lists the first three moods with examples (text cut off).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the five productive moods of the fourth figure by systematically varying the minor premise, and identify the first three moods as described in the text.

Core Idea
The five moods are derived by varying the minor premise and checking the condition.

The author explains that the four productive moods are derived by considering all possible forms of the minor premise (excluding particular negative) and matching them with compatible major premises. This yields five valid moods. In this lesson, we walk through the first three moods explicitly given in the text, using Islamic examples.

Guided Path
1
Recall the structure of the fourth figure
The middle term is the subject in the minor premise and the predicate in the major premise. This figure reverses the usual positions.
2
Identify possible minor premises
The minor can be universal affirmative, particular affirmative, or universal negative. Particular negative is impossible because it would combine particularity and negativity in one premise, violating the condition.
This gives three mini-cases.
3
Case 1: Minor universal affirmative
When the minor is universal affirmative, it can combine with three types of majors: universal affirmative, particular affirmative, and universal negative. These give three moods. The first mood is AA→I (both universal affirmatives, conclusion particular affirmative). The second mood is AI→I (minor universal affirmative, major particular affirmative, conclusion particular affirmative). The third mood is EA→O (minor universal negative, major universal affirmative) – but note the text's third mood is actually EA (minor negative, major affirmative), which fits as the third mood from the universal affirmative case? Actually the text lists the third mood as EA, which comes from the minor universal negative case. There is a slight inconsistency in the text's order; we follow the text's explicit listing.
The text's third mood is EA (both universal, minor negative, major affirmative).
4
Case 2: Minor particular affirmative
The minor particular affirmative can only combine with a universal negative major (the exception). This is the fourth mood: I,E → O.
This is the only case where both characteristics appear.
5
Case 3: Minor universal negative
The minor universal negative can only combine with a universal affirmative major. This is the fifth mood: E,A → O.
This completes the five moods.

The Five Productive Moods of the Fourth Figure
Author enumerates the five productive moods of the fourth figure with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the five productive moods of the fourth figure and verify the validity of a given categorical syllogism in that figure.

Core Idea
The fourth figure yields exactly five valid moods, each with unique premise combinations and conclusion types.

In this passage, the author enumerates the five productive moods of the fourth figure, where the middle term is subject in the minor and predicate in the major. Each mood is illustrated with examples using terms like 'human', 'animal', and 'rational'. Understanding these moods equips you to recognize valid syllogisms in this figure and to construct your own arguments.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the figure definition
The fourth figure has the middle term as subject in the minor premise and predicate in the major premise.
2
List the five moods
Note the five combinations of premises (affirmative/negative, universal/particular) that yield conclusions.
3
For each mood, identify the premises and conclusion
Using the examples, extract the two premises and the conclusion. Note the quantity and quality of each.
4
Verify validity
Check that each mood satisfies the conditions for a valid syllogism in the fourth figure (e.g., no two negative premises). The author asserts they are productive; confirm why.

فتبين أن الصغرى إن كانت موجبة كلية أنتجت مع ثلاث كبريات، وإن كانت جزئية موجبة أنتجت مع كبرى واحدة، وإن كانت كلية سالبة أنتجت مع كبرى واحدة كما أوضحنا . وهذه هي الضروب الخمسة المنتجة من الشكل الرابع، الذي هو ما كان الحد الوسط فيه موضوعا في الصغرى محمولاً في الكبرى : الضرب الأول موجبتان ،كليتان ينتج موجبة جزئية. ومثاله : كل إنسان حيوان وكل ناطق إنسان) ينتج : بعض الحيوان ناطق. الضرب الثاني: موجبتان صغراهما كلية وكبراهما جزئية، ينتج جزئية موجبة مثاله : (كل إنسان حيوان ، وبعض الناطق إنسان) ينتج : بعض الحيوان ناطق . الضرب الثالث : كليتان صغراهما سالبة وكبراهما موجبة، ينتج كلية سالبة مثاله : (لا شيء من الإنسان بفرس، وكل ناطق (إنسان) ينتج : (لا شيء من الفرس بناطق). الضرب الرابع موجبة كلية صغرى وسالبة كلية كبرى، ينتج جزئية سالبة . مثاله : (كل) إنسان حيوان ولا شيء من الفرس بإنسان) ينتج : (بعض الحيوان ليس بفرس). الضرب الخامس : موجبة جزئية صغرى وسالبة كلية كبرى وهي الصورة المستثناة من اجتماع الخستين - ينتج جزئية سالبة، مثاله : بعض الحيوان إنسان ولا شيء من الجماد بحيوان ينتج : (بعض الإنسان ليس بجماد).
It became clear that if the minor premise is universal affirmative, it produces with three major premises; if it is particular affirmative, it produces with one major premise; if it is universal negative, it produces with one major premise, as we have clarified. These are the five productive moods of the fourth figure, which is that in which the middle term is the subject in the minor premise and the predicate in the major premise: The first mood: both affirmatives, universal, produces a particular affirmative. Example: 'Every human is an animal, and every rational is human' yields 'Some animal is rational.' The second mood: both affirmatives, minor universal and major particular, produces a particular affirmative; example: 'Every human is an animal, and some rational being is human' yields 'Some animal is rational.' The third mood: both universal, minor negative and major affirmative, produces a universal negative; example: 'No human is a horse, and every rational is human' yields 'No horse is rational.' The fourth mood: universal affirmative minor and universal negative major, produces a particular negative; example: 'Every human is an animal, and no horse is human' yields 'Some animal is not a horse.' The fifth mood: particular affirmative minor and universal negative major—this is the form exempt from the two deficiencies—produces a particular negative; example: 'Some animal is human, and no inanimate is animal' yields 'Some human is not inanimate.'
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration and illustration of the five productive moods (ضروب منتجة) of the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism. The author begins by summarizing the conditions under which the minor premise (صغرى) yields conclusions with different major premises (كبرى). Then he lists the five moods, each with an example drawn from common knowledge (e.g., human, animal, rational, horse, inanimate). The first mood has two universal affirmatives, yielding a particular affirmative conclusion. The second mood has a universal affirmative minor and a particular affirmative major, also yielding a particular affirmative. The third mood has a universal negative minor and a universal affirmative major, yielding a universal negative. The fourth mood has a universal affirmative minor and a universal negative major, yielding a particular negative. The fifth mood is a special case ('الصورة المستثناة من اجتماع الخستين') where the minor is particular affirmative and the major is universal negative, yielding a particular negative. The author's examples follow the principle that all terms are meaningful within the Islamic sciences (e.g., 'rational' is a defining characteristic of humans). This systematic presentation helps the student memorize the valid moods of the fourth figure.

Key Terms
الشكل الرابع (al-shakl al-rabi'): The fourth figure of the categorical syllogism, where the middle term is the subject in the minor premise and the predicate in the major premise.
ضرب (darb): A mood of a syllogism, defined by the quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative) of its premises. [Note: In earlier logic, the term 'ضرب' often referred to a specific type of conditional syllogism; here it is used for categorical moods.]
الخستين (al-khisatayn): The two deficiencies (probably from 'khassa' meaning deficiency) in mood conditions; the fifth mood is exempt from these two drawbacks. [Note: This term is rare; not commonly used in standard mantiq texts.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which mood of the fourth figure produces a universal negative conclusion?
A) The first mood: both universal affirmatives
B) The second mood: universal affirmative minor and particular affirmative major
C) The third mood: universal negative minor and universal affirmative major
D) The fifth mood: particular affirmative minor and universal negative major

Key Takeaway
The fourth figure has exactly five productive moods, each defined by the quantity and quality of its premises, and they are all valid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Take a given syllogism like: 'Every wajib is good; some act is wajib; therefore some good is act.' Determine if it is a valid mood of the fourth figure and explain why or why not.

Why Some Ancients Omitted the Fourth Figure
Author mentions leaving aside later disagreements and notes that some ancients omitted the fourth figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why some ancient logicians omitted the fourth figure and why the author chooses to omit later debates on it.

Core Idea
The fourth figure was historically controversial; some omitted it as unnatural, yet later logicians expanded it.

Not every logical structure is accepted by all scholars. In this passage, the author explains why he leaves aside detailed debates about the fourth figure and notes that some early logicians dropped it altogether. This lesson will help you understand the historical and pedagogical reasoning behind these choices.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's stated reason for omitting later debates
The author says he left out the disagreement of later scholars (المتأخرون) concerning the fourth figure for brevity. His goal is to introduce the student to Adab al-Bahth, not to delve into every technical dispute.
2
Identify the condition for productivity debated by later scholars
Some later scholars said the fourth figure requires both premises affirmative, or that they differ in quality with one universal. This would yield eight productive moods instead of five.
This shows how later logicians refined the conditions.
3
Understand why some ancients omitted the fourth figure
Some ancient logicians omitted it because its conclusion is far from natural disposition (طبع). They only recognized three figures.
Natural disposition here means intuitive reasoning.
4
Note the reference to al-Ghazali
The author mentions that Abu Hamid al-Ghazali in 'Mi'yar al-'Ilm' followed the ancients in using only three figures. This shows the historical development of logic.
Al-Ghazali's choice influenced later Islamic thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنا تركنا خلاف المتأخرين في هذا الشكل، وقول جماعات منهم : إن شرط إنتاج هذا الشكل الرابع هو إيجاب مقدمتيه، أو اختلافهما في الكيف مع كلية إحداهما وبنوا على ذلك أن المنتج من ضروبه ثمانية تركنا ذلك كله اختصارًا؛ لأن مقصودنا بهذه المقدمة إيصال طالب العلم إلى إدراك فن آداب البحث والمناظرة، ولذلك تركنا كثيرًا من المناقشات في تقديم بعض الضروب على بعض في تعريف القياس وغيره اختصارًا كما أشرنا إليه في الترجمة . تنبيهات تتعلق بهذا الشكل الرابع : الأول : اعلم أن بعض الأقدمين حذفه ؛ لبعد إنتاجه عن الطبع، وجعل الأشكال ثلاثة (١) (۱) وعلى هذا جرى أبو حامد الغزالي في معيار العلم .
And know that we have left out the disagreement of later scholars concerning this figure, and the statement of groups among them that the condition for productivity of this fourth figure is the affirmation of both its premises, or their difference in quality with one of them being universal, and they built on that that the productive moods from it are eight; we left all that for brevity, because our purpose with this introduction is to bring the student of knowledge to the understanding of the art of Adab al-Bahth wal-Munazara, and therefore we left out many discussions about giving precedence to some moods over others in defining the syllogism and otherwise for brevity, as we indicated in the preface. Notifications pertaining to this fourth figure: The first: Know that some of the ancients omitted it because its production is far from natural disposition, and they made the figures three. (1) And upon this proceeded Abu Hamid al-Ghazali in Mi'yar al-'Ilm.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage is a methodological note and a historical observation. The author explains that he intentionally omits detailed disagreements among later logicians regarding the fourth figure, such as the condition for its productivity (some require both premises affirmative, others allow difference in quality with one universal), which would have resulted in eight moods instead of five. He does this for brevity, as his goal is to introduce the student to the discipline of Adab al-Bahth (dialectical debate). He then provides the first of several notifications: some ancient logicians omitted the fourth figure entirely because they found its conclusions counterintuitive (بعد إنتاجه عن الطبع), thus recognizing only three figures. He specifically cites al-Ghazali's 'Mi'yar al-'Ilm' as an example of this stance. This shows the author's awareness of the historical development of logic within the Islamic tradition and his pedagogical choice to simplify without completely ignoring important debates.

المتأخرون
الطبع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The fourth figure was not universally accepted; some early logicians omitted it because its conclusions are counterintuitive, and later refinements are often omitted in introductory texts for pedagogical reasons.

Examples of Moods in the Fourth Figure
Author gives examples of moods in the fourth figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the mood of any categorical syllogism in the fourth figure by determining the quantity and quality of each premise and the conclusion.

Core Idea
The fourth figure uses the middle term as predicate of the major and subject of the minor.

In this passage, the author demonstrates three valid moods of the fourth figure using concrete examples. By carefully parsing each syllogism, you will learn to identify the logical structure that makes these arguments valid. Pay close attention to how the minor and major premises combine to produce a particular type of conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the major and minor premises
In each example, determine which premise contains the predicate of the conclusion (major) and which contains the subject (minor). The minor premise typically has the same subject as the conclusion, and the major premise has the same predicate.
2
Extract the quantity and quality
For each premise and the conclusion, note whether it is universal (A or E) or particular (I or O), and affirmative (A or I) or negative (E or O). Use the letter codes: A = universal affirmative, E = universal negative, I = particular affirmative, O = particular negative.
3
Write the mood code
Combine the letters for minor premise, major premise, and conclusion in order (e.g., EAE). This code identifies the specific mood of the syllogism.
4
Verify the figure
Confirm that the middle term (the term that appears in both premises) is the predicate of the major premise and the subject of the minor premise. If so, the syllogism belongs to the fourth figure.
This step ensures you correctly recognized the figure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كلية سالبة مثاله : (لا شيء من الإنسان بفرس، وكل ناطق (إنسان) ينتج : (لا شيء من الفرس بناطق).
الضرب الرابع موجبة كلية صغرى وسالبة كلية كبرى، ينتج جزئية سالبة . مثاله : (كل) إنسان حيوان ولا شيء من الفرس بإنسان) ينتج : (بعض الحيوان ليس بفرس).
الضرب الخامس : موجبة جزئية صغرى وسالبة كلية كبرى وهي الصورة المستثناة من اجتماع الخستين - ينتج جزئية سالبة، مثاله : بعض الحيوان إنسان ولا شيء من الجماد بحيوان ينتج : (بعض الإنسان ليس بجماد).
Universal negative: example: 'No human is a horse, and every speaking is a human' yields 'No horse is speaking.' The fourth mood: universal affirmative minor and universal negative major, yields particular negative. Example: 'Every human is an animal and no horse is a human' yields 'Some animal is not a horse.' The fifth mood: particular affirmative minor and universal negative major, and it is the form excepted from the conjunction of the two [qualities], yields particular negative. Example: 'Some animal is a human and no inanimate is an animal' yields 'Some human is not inanimate.'
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage provides examples of three valid moods of the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism. The first unlabeled mood is a universal negative conclusion from a universal negative minor and universal affirmative major (EAE-4). The fourth mood is a particular negative (O) from a universal affirmative minor (A) and universal negative major (E), i.e., AEO-4. The fifth mood is a particular negative from a particular affirmative minor (I) and universal negative major (E), i.e., IEO-4. The author notes that the fifth mood is the form excepted from the conjunction of the two qualities, perhaps meaning it is the only mood where both premises are not universal. The examples use sensible categories like human, horse, animal, and inanimate (جماد) to illustrate logical relations.

Key Terms
الضرب (al-darb): A valid mood or combination of premises in a given syllogistic figure.
جزئية سالبة (juz'iyya saliba): A particular negative proposition (O type), e.g., 'Some A is not B.'
موجبة كلية (mujaba kulliyya): A universal affirmative proposition (A type).
سالبة كلية (saliba kulliyya): A universal negative proposition (E type).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The fourth figure's moods can be identified by the pattern of universal/particular and affirmative/negative in the premises and conclusion.

Three Notifications about the Fourth Figure
Author gives three observations on the fourth figure: its omission, its limited conclusions, and its reduction to the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why the fourth figure usually yields particular conclusions and explain the exceptional mood that yields a universal negative.

Core Idea
The fourth figure is mostly particular, but one mood yields a universal negative—and it reduces to the first.

This passage examines three key observations about the fourth figure of categorical syllogisms. You will learn why some ancient logicians omitted it, why it usually produces only particular conclusions, and how its unique valid mood works. You will also see how it can be reduced to the first figure, though the passage cuts off.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three notifications
List the three notifications: (1) Some ancients omitted the fourth figure entirely; (2) The figure yields only particular conclusions except in one mood; (3) It can be reduced to the first figure by swapping premises and contraposing the conclusion.
The third notification is incomplete in the source.
2
Analyze the structural reason for particularity
In the fourth figure, the middle term is subject of the minor and predicate of the major. The subject of the conclusion (predicate of minor) can be more general than the predicate of the conclusion (subject of major), so a universal judgment would not hold.
This explanation draws on the four relations (nisab arba') previously taught.
3
Examine the exceptional universal mood
When the minor is universal negative and the major universal affirmative (EAE-4), the conclusion is a universal negative. Work through the example: 'No human is a horse; every speaker is a human; therefore, no horse is a speaker.' The negative minor denies horseness of every human, and the affirmative major affirms humanity of every speaker, so speakerness is denied of every horse.
This is the only mood yielding a universal conclusion in the fourth figure.
4
Understand the reduction to the first figure
The author states that the fourth figure mostly reverts to the first figure by swapping the premises and contraposing the conclusion. The passage cuts off, but this technique shows that fourth-figure syllogisms are not fundamentally different.
Reduction is a common method in logic to simplify proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيهات تتعلق بهذا الشكل الرابع :
الأول : اعلم أن بعض الأقدمين حذفه ؛ لبعد إنتاجه عن الطبع، وجعل الأشكال ثلاثة
الثاني : اعلم أن سبب كون هذا الشكل لا ينتج إلا جزئية إلا في ضرب واحد من ضروبه هو أن الحد الوسط فيه هو موضوع الصغرى و محمول الكبرى والنتيجة فيه متركبة من محمول الصغرى وموضوع الكبرى، وموضوع نتيجته هو محمول الصغرى، ومحمولها هو موضوع الكبرى، ومن الجائز عقلاً أن يكون محمول الصغرى الذي هو موضوع النتيجة أعم من موضوع الكبرى الذي هو محمول النتيجة .
وإذا كان الموضوع أعم لم يصدق عليه الحكم بالمحمول إلا جزئيا، كما تقدم إيضاحه في النسب الأربع، وفي الكلام على الشكل الثالث
وإنما كانت نتيجته كلية فيما إذا تركب من كليتين صغراهما سالبة وكبراهما موجبة، كما [تقدّم ] (۳) في قولك: (لا شيء من الإنسان بفرس، وكل ناطق إنسان فإنه ينتج كلية سالبة هي : (لا شيء من الفرس بناطق)؛ لأن المقدمة الصغرى السالبة سلبت الفرسية عن كل فرد من أفراد الإنسان والمقدمة الكبرى أثبتت الإنسانية لكل ناطق، فلزم سلب الناطقية عن كل فرس كما ترى .
الثالث : هذا الشكل الرابع يرجع في الغالب إلى الشكل الأول : تارة بتبديل [مقدمتيه ](٤) مع عكس النتيجة، كالمتركب من كليتين
Notifications pertaining to this fourth figure:
First: Know that some of the ancients omitted it because its production is far from natural, and they made the figures three.
Second: Know that the reason this figure does not produce except a particular [conclusion] except in one of its moods is that the middle term in it is the subject of the minor and the predicate of the major, and the conclusion is composed of the predicate of the minor and the subject of the major, and the subject of its conclusion is the predicate of the minor, and its predicate is the subject of the major. And it is intellectually possible that the predicate of the minor, which is the subject of the conclusion, is more general than the subject of the major, which is the predicate of the conclusion. And if the subject is more general, the judgment of the predicate does not apply to it except partially, as was explained previously in the discussion of the four relations and in the speech on the third figure. And its conclusion is universal only when it is composed of two universal premises, the minor negative and the major affirmative, as [was presented] in your statement: 'No human is a horse, and every speaking is a human' — for it yields a universal negative: 'No horse is speaking'; because the minor negative premise negated horseness from every individual of human, and the major premise affirmed humanness for every speaking, so the negation of speakiness from every horse follows, as you see.
Third: This fourth figure mostly reverts to the first figure: sometimes by exchanging its two premises along with contraposition of the conclusion, like the composite of two universal [premises]...
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
The author presents three important observations about the fourth figure. First, ancient logicians (like al-Ghazali) omitted it entirely because its conclusions are unnatural and considered only three figures. Second, he explains the structural reason that the fourth figure yields only particular conclusions except in one mood: the middle term is the subject of the minor and predicate of the major, so the subject of the conclusion (predicate of minor) can be more general than the predicate of the conclusion (subject of major), causing a particular judgment. The exception is when the minor is universal negative and major universal affirmative (EAE-4), which yields a universal negative because the negative minor denies the major term of all subjects, and the affirmative major affirms the minor term of all middle, thus the conclusion is universal. Third, the figure often reduces to the first figure by swapping premises and contraposing the conclusion, but the passage is cut off. This explanation uses the example from the earlier mood.

Key Terms
الحد الوسط
موضوع
محمول
النسب الأربع
العكس

Key Takeaway
The fourth figure yields only particular conclusions except for the EAE mood, which gives a universal negative, and it can often be reduced to the first figure.

The Second Figure's Particularity and Its Exceptional Universal Conclusion
Author explains why the second figure yields particular conclusions except one case.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why the second figure of the categorical syllogism yields only particular conclusions, and recognize the one exceptional mood that yields a universal conclusion.

Core Idea
The second figure’s structural placement of the middle term limits its conclusions to particulars, except one precise combination.

The second figure of the categorical syllogism is known for its limitation: most of its moods produce only particular conclusions. Why is this so? In this passage, the author reveals the logical reason rooted in the position of the middle term and the relative scope of the terms. He also highlights the one exceptional mood where a universal conclusion emerges. Understanding this will sharpen your ability to evaluate syllogisms in Islamic theological and legal arguments.

Guided Path
1
Locate the middle term's position
In the second figure, the middle term is the subject of the minor premise and the predicate of the major premise. Identify this arrangement in the passage.
2
Identify the conclusion's components
The conclusion's subject comes from the predicate of the minor premise, and its predicate from the subject of the major premise. Note how the author describes this composition.
3
Explain why particularity is the norm
The passage argues that the predicate of the minor (subject of conclusion) can be more general than the subject of the major (predicate of conclusion), preventing a universal judgment. Understand this reasoning.
4
Find the exceptional mood
The author specifies one case where the conclusion is universal: when both premises are universal, the minor is negative and the major is affirmative. Locate this condition in the text.
5
Verify with the example
Walk through the example: 'No human is a horse; every rational being is human; therefore no horse is rational.' Confirm that it fits the exceptional mood and yields a universal negative conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : اعلم أن سبب كون هذا الشكل لا ينتج إلا جزئية إلا في ضرب واحد من ضروبه هو أن الحد الوسط فيه هو موضوع الصغرى و محمول الكبرى والنتيجة فيه متركبة من محمول الصغرى وموضوع الكبرى، وموضوع نتيجته هو محمول الصغرى، ومحمولها هو موضوع الكبرى، ومن الجائز عقلاً أن يكون محمول الصغرى الذي هو موضوع النتيجة أعم من موضوع الكبرى الذي هو محمول النتيجة . وإذا كان الموضوع أعم لم يصدق عليه الحكم بالمحمول إلا جزئيا، كما تقدم إيضاحه في النسب الأربع، وفي الكلام على الشكل الثالث وإنما كانت نتيجته كلية فيما إذا تركب من كليتين صغراهما سالبة وكبراهما موجبة، كما تقدم في قولك: (لا شيء من الإنسان بفرس، وكل ناطق إنسان فإنه ينتج كلية سالبة هي : (لا شيء من الفرس بناطق)؛ لأن المقدمة الصغرى السالبة سلبت الفرسية عن كل فرد من أفراد الإنسان والمقدمة الكبرى أثبتت الإنسانية لكل ناطق، فلزم سلب الناطقية عن كل فرس كما ترى .
Second: Know that the reason why this figure yields only particular [conclusions] except in one of its moods is that the middle term in it is the subject of the minor premise and the predicate of the major, and the conclusion in it is composed of the predicate of the minor and the subject of the major. The subject of its conclusion is the predicate of the minor, and its predicate is the subject of the major. It is intellectually possible that the predicate of the minor (which is the subject of the conclusion) be more general than the subject of the major (which is the predicate of the conclusion). And when the subject is more general, the judgment by the predicate does not apply to it except particularly, as previously clarified in the four relations and in the discussion on the third figure. Its conclusion is only universal when it is composed of two universal [premises] whose minor is negative and whose major is affirmative, as in your statement: 'No human is a horse, and every rational being is human' — it yields a universal negative: 'No horse is rational.' This is because the negative minor premise negated 'horseness' from every individual of humans, and the major premise affirmed 'humanness' for every rational being, so the negation of 'rationalness' from every horse follows, as you see.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory discussion of the second figure of the categorical syllogism. The author begins by stating the known fact that the second figure only yields particular conclusions except in one specific mood. He then gives the reason: the middle term is the subject of the minor and predicate of the major. As a result, the subject of the conclusion comes from the predicate of the minor, which may be more general than the predicate of the conclusion (which comes from the subject of the major). If the subject is more general, the predicate cannot be affirmed universally—only particularly. He references earlier clarifications on relative proportions. Then he identifies the exceptional case: when both premises are universal, the minor negative and major affirmative, the conclusion is universal negative. He illustrates with the example: 'No human is a horse; every rational being is human; therefore no horse is rational.' He explains why: the negative minor strips 'horseness' from all humans, and the major affirms 'humanness' for all rational beings, leading to the universal negative about horses. The explanation follows the logical structure precisely, showing how the combination yields certainty.

Reduction of the Fourth Figure to the First
Author explains reduction of fourth figure using example and poem.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reduce a fourth-figure categorical syllogism to the first figure by transposing premises and converting the conclusion.

Core Idea
Fourth figure reduction uses premise transposition plus conversion.

The fourth figure is the least common, but it can be transformed into the powerful first figure through simple operations. In this lesson, we follow the author's example to see how swapping the premises and converting the conclusion yields an equivalent first-figure syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fourth-figure mood
In the example, the premises are 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every rational being is a human' with conclusion 'Some animal is rational'. Note the middle term 'human' is predicate of the minor and subject of the major.
2
Swap the two premises
Exchange the minor and major premises. Now the minor becomes 'Every rational being is a human' and the major becomes 'Every human is an animal'. This arranges the middle term as subject of the minor and predicate of the major, which is the first figure.
The order of premises matters for figure identification.
3
Convert the conclusion
The new first-figure syllogism yields 'Every rational being is an animal'. By simple conversion, we obtain 'Some animal is rational', which matches the original fourth-figure conclusion.
Simple conversion swaps subject and predicate while preserving truth for particular affirmatives.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : هذا الشكل الرابع يرجع في الغالب إلى الشكل الأول : تارة بتبديل مقدمتيه مع عكس النتيجة، كالمتركب من كليتين موجبتين فلو قلت كل إنسان حيوان وكل ناطق إنسان فهذا هو الضرب الأول من الرابع، ونتيجته جزئية موجبة هي بعض الحيوان ناطق، فلو جعلت صغراه هي الكبرى وكبراه هي الصغرى، وعكست النتيجة بالعكس المستوي رجع إلى الأول . كما لو قلت : كل ناطق إنسان وكل إنسان حيوان ينتج من الشكل الأول كل ناطق حيوان، وهي عكس نتيجته قبل تبديل كل من المقدمتين بالأخرى. وتارة باستعمال العكس . والحاصل أن هذا الشكل ترجع ضروبه كلها للشكل الأول، إما بتبديل المقدمتين وعكس النتيجة، وإما باستعمال العكس المستوي في مقدمتيه، كما يدركه من فهم ما سبق، وليس قصدنا إطالة الكلام في ذلك ؛ لأن المقصود من هذه العجالة المنطقية هو التوصل إلى معرفة آداب البحث والمناظرة ونظم بعضهم وجه رد الأشكال الثلاثة إلى الشكل الأول بقوله : وغير أول من الأشكال إليه مردود بلا إشكال فالثان مردود بعكس الكبرى والثالث اردده بعكس الصغرى ورابع بعكس ترتيب يرد أو المقدمات هكذا ورد
Third: This fourth figure mostly reduces to the first figure: sometimes by swapping its two premises together with conversion of the conclusion, as in the compound of two universal affirmative [premises]. So if you say: 'Every human is an animal, and every rational being is a human' — this is the first mood of the fourth, and its conclusion is a particular affirmative: 'Some animal is rational.' If you make its minor the major and its major the minor, and convert the conclusion by simple conversion, it reduces to the first. For example, if you say: 'Every rational being is a human, and every human is an animal,' the first figure yields 'Every rational being is an animal,' which is the converse of the previous conclusion before swapping each of the two premises with the other. And sometimes by using conversion. The upshot is that all moods of this figure reduce to the first figure, either by swapping the premises and converting the conclusion, or by using simple conversion on its two premises, as understood by one who grasps what preceded. We do not intend to prolong the discussion in this, because the aim of this concise logical treatise is to attain knowledge of the etiquette of discussion and disputation. Someone composed a systematic summary of the reduction of the three figures to the first in his saying: 'Other than the first among the figures are reduced to it without doubt: The second is reduced by conversion of the major, the third — reduce it by conversion of the minor, and the fourth — by reversing the order it reduces, or the premises are thus reduced.'
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage covers the reduction of the fourth figure to the first and a poetic summary for all non-first figures. The author first explains two methods of reduction: (1) swapping the minor and major premises and then converting the conclusion via simple conversion, and (2) using simple conversion on one or both premises. He illustrates with a fourth-figure mood consisting of two universal affirmatives: 'Every human is an animal; every rational being is a human' yields the particular conclusion 'Some animal is rational.' After swapping premises (so the minor becomes 'Every rational being is a human' and the major 'Every human is an animal'), the first figure gives 'Every rational being is an animal,' which is the simple converse of the original conclusion. Alternatively, conversion of premises alone can work. The author then states that all moods reduce to the first via these methods. He concludes by citing a poem (nazm) that succinctly states the reduction methods for the three non-first figures: the second by conversion of the major premise, the third by conversion of the minor, and the fourth either by reversing order (transposition of premises) or by converting the premises (the wording is ambiguous but suggests both). The poem serves as a mnemonic for the student.

The Mnemonic Poem for Figure Reduction
Poem summarizes reduction methods for all figures to first.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to recall the reduction method for each of the three non-first figures using the poem's mnemonic.

Core Idea
A poem encodes reduction methods for all non-first figures.

To help memorize the reduction techniques, the author cites a poem that succinctly states how the second, third, and fourth figures are reduced to the first. This lesson breaks down the poem line by line.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three non-first figures mentioned in the poem
The poem names the second (thānī), third (thālith), and fourth (rābiʿ) figures. The first figure is the target of reduction.
2
Recall the reduction for the second figure
Poem says 'fathānī mardūd bi-ʿaks al-kubrā' – the second is reduced by conversion of the major premise. That means to get a first-figure syllogism from the second, convert the major premise.
Conversion must be simple conversion for universal negative and particular affirmative.
3
Recall the reduction for the third figure
Poem says 'wa-ththālithu ordudhu bi-ʿaks al-sughrā' – the third is reduced by conversion of the minor premise.
4
Recall the reduction for the fourth figure
Poem says 'wa-rābiʿ bi-ʿaks tartībin yuruddu aw al-muqaddimātuhā kadhā rudda' – the fourth is reduced either by reversing the order (transposition of premises) or by converting the premises. The 'aks tartīb refers to swapping premises.
