Productive and Unproductive Modes in Man'iat al-Jam'
Author explains the four modes of disjunctive syllogism with mutual exclusivity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish productive from unproductive modes in a mutually exclusive disjunctive syllogism and apply them correctly in Islamic legal reasoning.

Core Idea
In mutual exclusivity, only affirmation yields denial—denial yields nothing.

This passage from adab al-bahth clarifies which inferences are valid in disjunctive syllogisms where options cannot both be true. Understanding these modes prevents common logical errors in Islamic legal and theological reasoning, especially when evaluating mutually exclusive rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunction type
Recognize that the passage discusses 'man'iat al-jam' (mutual exclusivity only), not exhaustive disjunction. The disjuncts cannot coexist but may both be absent.
2
Recognize the two productive modes
Affirming one exact disjunct (istithnā' 'ayn al-muqaddam or al-tāli) yields the negation of the other. Reason: the two are incompatible in existence.
These are al-muntijān.
3
Recognize the two unproductive modes
Affirming the negation of one disjunct (i.e., denying it) does not yield the other. Reason: the negation of one could be true when the other is false, as other possibilities exist.
These are al-'aqīmān.
4
Test with the example
Consider the body example: white/black. Affirming white yields not black; affirming black yields not white. But denying white (saying 'not white') could mean black or another color, so no conclusion.
Apply this structure to any mutually exclusive pair in Islamic sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالمنتجان منها :هما استثناء عين المقدم، فإنه ينتج نقيض التالي، واستثناء عين التالي، فإنه ينتج نقيض المقدم؛ للعناد بين طرفيها في الوجود. والضربان العقيمان هما استثناء نقيض التالي، واستثناء نقيض المقدم، فلا ينتجان ؛ لعدم العناد بين طرفيها في العدم. فلو قلت: (الجسم إما أبيض وإما أسود، لكنه أبيض) أنتج : فهو غير أسود) . ولو قلت : (لكنه أسود) أنتج : (فهو غير أبيض). ولو قلت : ( لكنه غيرُ أبيض) لم ينتج شيئاً؛ لصدق (غير الأبيض) بالأسود وبغيره.
The two productive ones are: affirming the exact antecedent, for it yields the negation of the consequent, and affirming the exact consequent, for it yields the negation of the antecedent, because of the opposition between the two extremes in existence. The two unproductive types are: affirming the negation of the consequent, and affirming the negation of the antecedent, for they do not yield anything, because there is no opposition between the two extremes in non-existence. So if you say: 'The body is either white or black, but it is white,' it yields: 'So it is not black.' And if you say: 'But it is black,' it yields: 'So it is not white.' And if you say: 'But it is not white,' it does not yield anything, because 'not white' applies to black and to other things.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage discusses the disjunctive syllogism of the type man'iat al-jam', where the two disjuncts are mutually exclusive (cannot both be true). The author specifies two productive modes: affirming one disjunct yields denying the other; and affirming the other yields denying the first. The reason is the opposition (عناد) between the two in existence. The two unproductive modes are denying one disjunct (i.e., affirming its negation) to conclude the other; this fails because the disjuncts are not exhaustive, so the negation of one does not entail the other. The example with 'white or black' illustrates: if it is white, it is not black; if it is black, it is not white. But if it is not white, it could be black or another color, so no conclusion.

Key Terms

نقيض
المنتجان
العقيمان
العناد

D) Denying both disjuncts.

Key Takeaway
In a mutually exclusive disjunction, only affirming one disjunct yields the negation of the other; denying one disjunct yields no conclusion.

Rules of the Disjunctive Syllogism of Man'iat al-Khuluw al-Mujawwizah lil-Jam'
Author describes the productive and unproductive modes for the special disjunction that permits conjunction, reversing the rules of the previous type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the productive and unproductive modes in a disjunctive syllogism composed of man'iat al-khuluw al-mujawwizah lil-jam' and apply them to Islamic examples.

Core Idea
In a disjunction that permits conjunction, denying one side proves the other, but affirming one side proves nothing.

This lesson introduces a special type of disjunctive syllogism where both disjuncts can be true simultaneously. The rules are the reverse of the previous type: here, affirming the negation of one disjunct yields the other, while affirming the exact disjunct yields nothing. Understanding this reversal is crucial for accurate reasoning in Islamic dialectics and legal theory, where such disjunctions frequently occur.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunction type
The passage describes 'man'iat al-khuluw al-mujawwizah lil-jam' – a disjunction that permits both disjuncts to be true together (inclusive OR). Contrast this with 'man'iat al-jam' (exclusive OR) where both cannot be true.
The term is non-standard; typically 'man'iat al-khuluw' means at least one must be true, and the addition 'al-mujawwizah lil-jam' emphasizes that both may be true.
2
Recognize the reversal of rules
The author states that the two modes that were unproductive in the previous type (man'iat al-jam') are now productive, and vice versa. In man'iat al-jam', affirming one disjunct yields the negation of the other; here, affirming the negation of one yields the other.
This reversal is due to the logical properties of inclusive vs. exclusive disjunction.
3
State the productive and unproductive modes
Productive modes: affirming the negation (naqīḍ) of either extreme yields the other extreme. Unproductive modes: affirming the exact (ʿayn) of either extreme yields nothing. The contradictory of negation is affirmation, and vice versa.
The author uses the terms 'al-ḍarbān al-ʿaqīmān' (unproductive) and 'al-ḍarbān al-muntijān' (productive).
4
Apply to the example
Consider the proposition: 'The body is either not-white or not-black.' (1) If we affirm the negation of the first disjunct (i.e., 'it is white'), we conclude 'it is not-black' because white is necessarily not-black. (2) If we affirm the negation of the second disjunct (i.e., 'it is black'), we conclude 'it is not-white'. (3) Affirming the exact disjunct 'it is not-black' yields no conclusion, because 'not-black' is true of white and also of other colors.
The example uses negative predicates to illustrate the logical form; the same pattern applies with positive predicates.
5
Generalize the rule
In any disjunction of this type, the valid inferences are: (A) assert the contradictory of disjunct 1 → conclude disjunct 2; (B) assert the contradictory of disjunct 2 → conclude disjunct 1. Asserting a disjunct itself yields no necessary conclusion.
This matches the inclusive OR truth table: if one disjunct is false, the other must be true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

3. Thus, affirming the negation of each extreme in it yields the other extreme itself, and affirming the exact each extreme yields nothing. And you know that the contradictory of negation is affirmation, and the contradictory of affirmation is negation. So if you say: 'The body is either not-white or not-black' and you affirm the negation of the antecedent by saying: 'But it is white', it yields the consequent itself, which is 'not-black', because white is necessarily not-black. And similarly, if you affirm the negation of the consequent by saying: 'But it is black', it yields the antecedent itself, which is 'not-white', because black is necessarily not-white. And similarly, if you affirm the exact consequent by saying: 'But it is not-black', it yields nothing, because 'not-black' is true of white and of not-white, etc. And this summary is what we wanted to mention before embarking on the discipline of dialectics and disputation; because the discipline of dialectics and disputation is not understood except by one who has familiarity with what we have mentioned in this summary. Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage introduces a special type of disjunctive syllogism: man'iat al-khuluw al-mujawwizah lil-jam'. The author explains that the rules are the reverse of the previous type (man'iat al-jam'). Here, the two modes that were unproductive before become productive: affirming the negation of either disjunct yields the other disjunct itself. Conversely, affirming the exact disjunct yields nothing. The example uses negative disjuncts: 'either not-white or not-black'. If we assert 'white' (the negation of 'not-white'), we conclude 'not-black', because white is necessarily not-black. Similarly, asserting 'black' yields 'not-white'. But asserting 'not-black' yields nothing, because 'not-black' includes both white and other colors. The author states that this summary is necessary for understanding adab al-bahth (the discipline of dialectics). The passage ends with a note on the curriculum.

Key Terms

The productive rule is: affirming the negation of one yields the other. [Note: Non-standard term; typically 'man'iat al-khuluw' is used for inclusive OR.]
نقيض النفي (naqīḍ al-nafy): The contradictory of negation, i.e., affirmation.
الضربان العقيمان (al-ḍarbān al-'aqīmān): The two unproductive modes (affirming the exact disjuncts) in this context.
الضربان المنتجان (al-ḍarbān al-muntijān): The two productive modes (affirming the negation of each disjunct).

Key Takeaway
In a disjunctive syllogism that permits conjunction (man'iat al-khuluw al-mujawwizah lil-jam'), affirming the negation of one disjunct yields the other, while affirming the exact disjunct yields nothing.

Rules of Conditional Syllogism with Disjunctive Premises
Author illustrates rules of conditional syllogism using a disjunction about body and color.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the three istithna' operations on a disjunctive conditional premise and determine whether the conclusion follows necessarily.

Core Idea
Only excluding the opposite of a disjunct yields a necessary conclusion; excluding the disjunct itself does not.

The conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i) is a cornerstone of logical inference in Islamic scholarship, particularly for adab al-bahth. In this lesson, we examine a disjunctive conditional premise and test which exclusions produce valid conclusions, following the author's concrete example. By mastering these rules, you will be able to evaluate arguments in usul al-fiqh and kalam where jurists and theologians reason with alternatives.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive premise
Locate the disjunction in the Arabic: 'al-jismu imma ghayru abyad wa imma ghayru aswad' (the body is either not-white or not-black). Note that it is a disjunction of two negatives.
This is a man'at al-khuluw (preventing emptiness) type disjunction: both cannot be false together.
2
Apply the first istithna': exclude the opposite of the antecedent
The author says: 'wa istathnayta naqida al-muqaddam fa qulta: lakin-nahu abyad' (and you exclude the opposite of the antecedent, saying: but it is white). Since white is the opposite of 'not-white', the antecedent becomes false, forcing the consequent 'not-black' to be true. The conclusion 'ghayru aswad' follows necessarily.
This corresponds to modus tollens on the first disjunct, effectively denying one alternative.
3
Apply the second istithna': exclude the opposite of the consequent
The author then excludes the opposite of the consequent: 'lakin-nahu aswad' (but it is black). Black is the opposite of 'not-black', so the consequent is false, forcing the antecedent 'not-white' to be true. Conclusion: 'ghayru abyad' follows.
Again, modus tollens on the second disjunct.
4
Apply the third istithna': exclude exactly the consequent
Finally, the author excludes exactly the consequent: 'lakin-nahu ghayru aswad' (but it is not-black). This does not force a conclusion because 'not-black' is true for both white and non-white colors (e.g., red, green). The disjunction remains unresolved.
This shows that affirming a disjunct does not yield a necessary conclusion; only denying one does (by modus tollens).
5
Generalize the rule
From the three tests, the rule emerges: In a disjunctive conditional, the valid inference is to exclude (istithna') the opposite of a disjunct, which yields the other disjunct. Excluding a disjunct itself gives no necessary conclusion unless the disjunction is exclusive (mani'at al-jam', but that is not the case here).
This is the foundation for more complex conditional reasoning in adab al-bahth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلو قلت : (الجسم إما غيرُ أبيض وإما غير أسود) واستثنيت نقيض المقدم فقلت : لكنه أبيض) أنتج عين التالي، الذي هو (غير أسود)؛ لأن الأبيض غير أسود بالضرورة. وكذلك لو استثنيت نقيض التالي فقلت: (لكنه أسود) أنتج عين المقدم، الذي هو ( غير أبيض) ؛ لأن الأسود غير أبيض ضرورة . وكذلك لو استثنيت عين التالي فقلت : (لكنه غير أسود) لم يُنتج شيئًا ؛ لأن غير الأسود صادق بالأبيض وغير الأبيض، وهكذا. وهذه العجالة هي التي أردنا أن نذكرها قبل الشروع في أدب البحث والمناظرة ؛ لأن أدب البحث والمناظرة لا يفهمه إلا من له إلمام بما ذكرنا في هذه العجالة. (وبالله التوفيق). وبهذا ينتهي مقرر السنة الأولى وسيأتي مقرر السنة الثانية من قواعد و مباحث أدب البحث والمناظرة إن شاء الله - تعالى -.
If you say: (The body is either not-white or not-black) and you exclude the opposite of the antecedent, so you say: (but it is white), then it produces exactly the consequent, which is (not-black); because white is necessarily not-black. Similarly, if you exclude the opposite of the consequent, so you say: (but it is black), it produces exactly the antecedent, which is (not-white); because black is necessarily not-white. And similarly, if you exclude exactly the consequent, so you say: (but it is not-black), it produces nothing; because not-black is true for white and not-white, and so on. And this summary is what we intended to mention before commencing on the etiquette of inquiry and debate; because the etiquette of inquiry and debate is not understood except by one who has familiarity with what we have mentioned in this summary. (And success is from Allah). With this concludes the syllabus of the first year, and the syllabus of the second year from the rules and topics of the etiquette of inquiry and debate will follow, God willing - the Exalted.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage explains the rules of the conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i) applied to a disjunctive premise. The author sets up a disjunction: 'The body is either not-white or not-black.' Then he tests three different 'exclusions' (istithna'). First, he excludes the opposite of the antecedent, affirming 'it is white.' Since white makes the first alternative (not-white) false, the second alternative (not-black) must be true, so the conclusion is 'it is not-black.' Second, he excludes the opposite of the consequent, affirming 'it is black.' This makes the second alternative false, so the first alternative (not-white) is concluded. Third, he excludes exactly the consequent, affirming 'it is not-black.' This produces no necessary conclusion because 'not-black' is true for both white and non-white (e.g., red, green). Thus the inference fails. The author emphasizes that this summary is essential preparation for studying adab al-bahth wal-munazara (the etiquette of inquiry and debate), which requires familiarity with these logical foundations. The passage ends with a note about the syllabus for the first and second years.

التالي
نقيض
استثناء
نتج

Key Takeaway
In a disjunctive conditional syllogism, only excluding (istithna') the opposite of a disjunct yields the other disjunct necessarily; excluding the disjunct itself yields nothing unless the disjunction is exclusive.

The Method of Sabr wa-Taqsim as a Tasdiqi Proof
Definition of sabr wa-taqsim as a tasdiqi proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two components of a sabr wa-taqsim proof and explain how they combine to produce a tasdiqi assent.

Core Idea
Sabr wa-taqsim proves truth by exhaustively dividing possibilities and eliminating false ones.

In this passage, the author defines sabr wa-taqsim as a kind of assertoric proof. He explains that it consists of a sound rational division (taqsim) that exhausts all possibilities, followed by a testing (sabr) that distinguishes valid from invalid. When both are sound, the combination yields certainty. This method is also called the disjunctive conditional by logicians.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parts of the proof
The passage states that if the division is sound and the testing is sound, then the two together are a tasdiqi proof. Part one is the division (taqsim), part two is the testing (sabr).
2
Understand the role of division
Division must fulfill its conditions: it must be exhaustive and rational, often by dichotomy (a thing and its negation). The division is the first part of the proof.
The passage says 'the division is part of it'.
3
Understand the role of sabr
Sabr is the examination of each division to determine which option is valid (sahih) and which is false (fasid). It is the second part.
The author calls it 'testing' and 'examining'.
4
Connect to tasdiq
When both division and sabr are sound, they together produce assent (tasdiq) to a proposition. Hence the combination is a tasdiqi proof.
The author explicitly says 'the two mentioned things constitute an assertoric proof'.

صح التقسيم واستوفى شروطه المذكورة وصح سبرها وهو اختبار صحيحها من فاسدها، وصالحها من غيره كان الأمران المذكوران دليلاً تصديقيًا، والتقسيم جزء منه . وهذا الدليل الذي هو السبر والتقسيم يستعمله المنطقيون في مقاصد أخر ، وهو ما قدمنا أنهم يسمونه الشرطي المنفصل واعلم أنا قد بسطنا الكلام في هذا الدليل في كتابنا «أضواء البيان في إيضاح القرآن بالقرآن في سورة مريم، في الكلام على قوله - تعالى -: ﴿ أَطَّلَعَ الْغَيْبَ أَمِ اتَّخَذَ عِندَ الرَّحْمَنِ عَهْدًا، وبينا المراد به عند الأصوليين والجدليين والمنطقيين، وأوضحنا الفرق بين ما تريد به كل منهم وبين ما تريد به الأخرى، وذكرنا له أربعة أمثلة قرآنية، وبينا بعض آثاره التاريخية العقائدية والأدبية، ولذلك سنختصر الكلام عليه هنا، فنذكر له مثالاً في القرآن ومثالاً في الأصول، ومثالاً في الجدل. فمن أمثلته في القرآن قوله - تعالى -: ﴿ أَمْ خُلِقُوا مِنْ غَيْرِ شَيْءٍ أَمْ هُمُ الْخَلِقُونَ ﴾ [الطور / ٣٥] . وقد قدمنا أن التقسيم في هذه الآية عقلي، وإنما كانت الأقسام ثلاثة لأنهما تقسيمان عقليان، قطعيان لأنا نقول : إما أن يكونوا خُلقوا بلا خالق أصلاً ، أو خلقهم خالق، وهذا تقسيم عقلي قطعي ؛ لحصره في الشيء ونقيضه، ثم نقسم القسم الثاني الذي هو قولنا : أو خلقهم خالق، فنقول : لا يخلو ذلك الخالق من أن يكون أنفسهم أو ليس بأنفسهم، وهذا أيضًا تقسيم عقلي قطعي ؛ لحصره في الشيء ونقيضه، فصارت الأقسام في التقسيمين ثلاثة : الأول كونهم خلقوا بلا خالق أصلاً ، الثاني أنهم خَلقوا أنفسهم الثالث أنهم خلقهم خالق غيرُ أنفسهم. فهذا التقسيم العقلي الصحيح جزء الدليل التصديقي، وجزءه الآخر هو سبر هذه الأقسام الثلاثة واختبارها، وتمييز الصحيح منها من الباطل. وبالسبر الصحيح يتبين أن القسمين الأولين ـ وهو أنهم خُلقوا بدون خالق أو أنهم خلقوا أنفسهم - باطلان غاية البطلان كما لا يخفى ، وأن القسم الثالث - وهو أنهم خلقهم خالق - صحيح لاشك فيه، وهذا الخالق الذي خلقهم هو خالق السموات والأرض ومن فيهما ـ سبحانه وتعالى علوا كبيرًا .. فالقسم الصحيح من الأقسام حذف في الآية؛ لدلالة المقام عليه، فدل التقسيم والسبر المذكوران على أن لهم خالقا هو الذي خلقهم، وهو الذي يستحق عليهم أن يعبدوه وحده، فكونهم خلقهم خالق تصديق، وكان التقسيم جزء الدليل عليه، ومن المعلوم أن الحصر والتقسيم إن كانا قطعيين كان السبر والتقسيم دليلاً قطعيًا، كالآية التي مثلنا بها، فدلالتها على أنه - تعالى - هو الخالق المستحق للعبادة وحده قطعية ؛ لأن الحصر والإبطال [فيها] قطعيان . واعلم أن الأصوليين إنما يستعملون هذا الدليل في إثبات العلة أو صف المحل، وإبطال غير الصالح للتعليل، فيتعين الباقي بحصر الصالح للتعليل . كقول الشافعي : علة تحريم الربا في البر إما الكيل وإما الطعم وإما الاقتيات والادخار مثلاً، ثم يستدل على بطلان وصف الكيل ووصف الاقتيات والادخار بأن ملء الكف من البر لا يجوز فيه الربا، مع أنه غير
The division is sound and fulfills its mentioned conditions, and its testing (sabr) is sound—which is examining its valid from its invalid, and its suitable from others—then the two mentioned things constitute an assertoric proof (dalil tasdiqi), and the division is part of it. And this proof, which is sabr wa-taqsim, is used by logicians for other purposes, and it is what we previously stated they call the disjunctive conditional (al-sharti al-munfasil). Know that we have expanded the discussion on this proof in our book 'Adwa' al-Bayan fi Idah al-Qur'an bi al-Qur'an' in Surah Maryam, regarding His statement, Exalted: 'Has he looked into the unseen or taken a covenant with the Most Merciful?' and we explained its intended meaning according to the usuliyyun, jadaliyyun, and mantiqiyyun, and clarified the difference between what each of them intends by it and what the other intends, and we mentioned four Qur'anic examples for it, and explained some of its historical, creedal, and literary effects. Therefore we will shorten the discussion on it here, and mention one example from the Qur'an, one from usul, and one from jadal. Among its examples in the Qur'an is His statement, Exalted: 'Or were they created from nothing, or are they the creators?' [Q. 52:35]. And we have stated that the division in this verse is rational, and the divisions are three because they are two rational, certain divisions. For we say: either they were created with no creator at all, or a creator created them—and this is a rational, certain division because it exhausts a thing and its negation. Then we divide the second division, which is our saying: 'or a creator created them,' and we say: that creator is not free from being themselves or not being themselves—and this is also a rational, certain division because it exhausts a thing and its negation. Thus the divisions in the two divisions become three: first, that they were created with no creator at all; second, that they created themselves; third, that a creator other than themselves created them. So this sound rational division is part of the assertoric proof, and its other part is the testing (sabr) of these three divisions and examining them, distinguishing the sound from the false. By the sound testing it becomes clear that the first two divisions—namely, that they were created without a creator or that they created themselves—are extremely false, as is not hidden, and that the third division—namely, that a creator created them—is sound, no doubt about it. And this creator who created them is the Creator of the heavens and the earth and what is in them—glorified and exalted be He, a great exaltation. The sound division from among the divisions was omitted in the verse because the context indicates it. So the mentioned division and testing indicate that they have a Creator who created them, and He is the one who deserves that they worship Him alone. Thus that a Creator created them is an assent (tasdiq), and the division was part of the proof for it. And it is known that if the enumeration (hasr) and division are certain, then the sabr wa-taqsim is a certain proof, like the verse we have exemplified. So its indication that He, Exalted, is the Creator deserving of worship alone is certain because the enumeration and nullification in it are certain. And know that the usuliyyun only use this proof to establish the 'illa (cause) of the attributes of the subject and to nullify what is unsuitable for ta'lil (legal reasoning), so the remaining is determined by the enumeration of what is suitable for ta'lil. Like the statement of al-Shafi'i: the cause of the prohibition of riba in wheat is either measurement by weight or by volume (al-kayl) or edibility (al-ta'm) or storable sustenance (al-iqtitat wa al-iddikhar) for example, then he argues for the nullity of the attribute of measurement and the attribute of storable sustenance by the fact that a handful of wheat does not permit riba in it, even though it is not
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage explains the logical method of sabr wa-taqsim (testing and division) as a type of assertoric proof (dalil tasdiqi). The author begins by stating that if a division fulfills its conditions and its testing (sabr) that distinguishes valid from invalid is sound, then the two together constitute a proof, with division as a part. This proof is also called the disjunctive conditional (sharti munfasil) by logicians. The author refers to an earlier expanded treatment in his work 'Adwa' al-Bayan' and now summarizes, giving three examples: one from the Qur'an, one from usul al-fiqh, and one from jadal. The Qur'anic example is Q. 52:35: 'Or were they created from nothing, or are they the creators?' The author analyzes this verse as containing two rational, certain divisions. First, humans are either created without a creator or by a creator. This is certain because it exhausts a thing and its negation. Second, if created by a creator, that creator is either themselves or another. This is also certain for the same reason. Thus three possibilities emerge: no creator, self-created, or created by another. The sabr (testing) then examines these: the first two are false (absurd), so the third is true—they are created by a Creator, who is Allah. The verse omits the third possibility because context indicates it. The author emphasizes that if the enumeration (hasr) and division are certain, the whole proof is certain. The verse's indication is certain because both the exhaustive enumeration and the nullification of false options are certain. The author then mentions how usuliyyun (legal theorists) use this method: they establish the 'illa (legal cause) by listing possible attributes of a subject (e.g., for riba in wheat: measurement by volume, edibility, storable sustenance), then test and eliminate those that do not hold in all cases. An example from Imam al-Shafi'i is given: he posits that the cause for riba in wheat is either measurement (al-kayl), edibility (al-ta'm), or storable sustenance (al-iqtitat wa al-iddikhar). Then he nullifies measurement and storable sustenance by pointing out that a handful of wheat (which is not precisely measured) still involves riba, so those attributes are not the cause, leaving edibility as the correct 'illa. This passage demonstrates the power of sabr wa-taqsim as a tool for certainty in both theology and jurisprudence.

Key Terms
سبر (sabr): Testing or examining each division (qism) after a logical division to determine which is valid and which is invalid; a method of elimination. [Note: In early logic, sabr was less formalized; later usul al-fiqh developed it as a key method for 'illa verification.]
تقسيم (taqsim): A rational division that exhausts all possibilities, often by dichotomy (thing and its negation), used as the first part of the proof. [Note: No significant shift; stable across logic and usul.]
دليل تصديقي (dalīl taṣdīqī): A proof that establishes assent (tasdiq) to a proposition, as opposed to a conceptual definition (tasawwur). Here, the combination of division and testing yields a tasdiqi proof. [Note: In later Arabic logic, tasdiqi proofs are often equated with syllogisms; this passage extends it to sabr wa-taqsim.]
حصر (ḥaṣr): Exhaustive enumeration of all possibilities in a division, ensuring certainty if the division is dichotomous. [Note: Stable term; used in mantiq and usul.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what two things together constitute an assertoric proof (dalil tasdiqi)?
A) A sound premise and a sound conclusion
B) A sound division and a sound testing (sabr)
C) A sound definition and a sound syllogism
D) A sound enumeration and a sound nullification

Key Takeaway
Sabr wa-taqsim is a two-step process: exhaustive division followed by elimination of false options, yielding a certain conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the definition provided, construct a simple sabr wa-taqsim proof for the theological proposition: 'The Quran is the word of Allah.' List the exhaustive possibilities (e.g., it is the word of Allah, it is the word of a human, it is the word of a jinn) and then test each to eliminate the false options.

The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
The Qur'anic Example of Sabr wa-Taqsim: Proving the Creator
Qur'anic example of sabr wa-taqsim (Q. 52:35)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze Q. 52:35 as a sabr wa-taqsim proof and identify the two dichotomous divisions that produce three possibilities.

Core Idea
The Qur'an tests the disbelievers' claim by systematically dividing possibilities and eliminating absurdities.

The author analyzes Q. 52:35 to demonstrate sabr wa-taqsim. The verse asks: 'Or were they created from nothing, or are they the creators?' This implies a division. The author shows that there are two rational divisions that create three possibilities. Through testing, the false options (no creator or self-creation) are eliminated, leaving the truth: they were created by Allah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first division
The passage says: either they were created without a creator or by a creator. This is a dichotomy (thing and its negation).
This division is rational and certain.
2
Identify the second division
If by a creator, that creator is either themselves or another. This is also a dichotomy.
This subdivision applies only to the second branch of the first division.
3
List the three resulting possibilities
The combination yields three exhaustive possibilities: (a) no creator, (b) self-created, (c) created by another Creator.
The author notes that the divisions are two but yield three options.
4
Apply sabr (testing)
Test each possibility. The first two are absurd: nothing cannot create, and self-creation is impossible. The third is necessarily true.
The author says the first two are 'extremely false'.
5
Observe the omitted sound division
The verse omits the third option because the context indicates it. The division and testing prove that a Creator exists.
The omission is rhetorical, not logical.

This passage explains the logical method of sabr wa-taqsim (testing and division) as a type of assertoric proof (dalil tasdiqi). Key Terms
سبر (sabr): Testing or examining each division (qism) after a logical division to determine which is valid and which is invalid; a method of elimination. [Note: In early logic, sabr was less formalized; later usul al-fiqh developed it as a key method for 'illa verification.]

Checkpoint
In the Qur'anic example (Q. 52:35), what are the two rational, certain divisions that lead to three possibilities?
A) Division into created by Allah or created by chance; then division into chance or necessity.
B) Division into created with a creator or without; then if with a creator, that creator is themselves or another.
C) Division into nothing or something; then division into something or everything.
D) Division into good or evil; then division into just or unjust.

Key Takeaway
The Qur'anic sabr wa-taqsim yields a certain proof of Allah's existence because the division is exhaustive and the false options are self-evidently absurd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take another Qur'anic verse that uses a similar rhetorical question (e.g., Q. 56:58-59 'Have you seen that which you emit? Is it you who create it, or are We the Creator?') and analyze it as a sabr wa-taqsim proof. Identify the implicit divisions and the testing that eliminates false options.

Sabr wa-Taqsim in Usul al-Fiqh: Determining the 'Illa
Usul al-fiqh example of sabr wa-taqsim (al-Shafi'i on riba)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the method of sabr wa-taqsim to determine the 'illa of a legal ruling using al-Shafi'i's example on riba.

Core Idea
In usul al-fiqh, sabr wa-taqsim isolates the true cause of a ruling by eliminating all unsuitable attributes.

The author explains how legal theorists (usuliyyun) use sabr wa-taqsim to identify the 'illa (effective cause) of a ruling. He cites al-Shafi'i's investigation into the cause of the prohibition of riba in wheat. Al-Shafi'i lists possible attributes and then tests each: if an attribute is present in a case where the ruling does not apply, it is invalid. Through elimination, only the correct cause remains.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and ruling
The subject is wheat (burr) and the ruling is the prohibition of riba (interest) in it.
The passage mentions the ruling as 'tahrim al-riba fi al-burr'.
2
List the candidate ʿillat
The candidate attributes are: al-kayl (measurement by volume), al-ta'm (edibility), and al-iqtitat wa al-iddikhar (storable sustenance). These are the possible causes.
The text says 'الكيل أو الطعم أو الاقتيات والادخار'.
3
Test each candidate using a counterexample
Al-Shafi'i nullifies al-kayl and al-iqtitat wa al-iddikhar by pointing to a handful of wheat: it is not precisely measured, yet riba still applies (so measurement fails). The reason for nullifying storable sustenance is not given in the passage, but the principle is similar.
The passage cuts off, but the method is clear.
4
Conclude the correct ʿilla
After elimination, only al-ta'm (edibility) remains, so edibility is the true cause of the prohibition.
This is the surviving attribute.
5
Understand the requirement of exhaustive enumeration
The division must include all possible attributes; otherwise, the remaining option may not be the correct cause. Incomplete enumeration weakens the proof.
The author earlier stressed that the enumeration must be certain.

باطلان غاية البطلان كما لا يخفى ، وأن القسم الثالث - وهو أنهم خلقهم خالق - صحيح لاشك فيه، وهذا الخالق الذي خلقهم هو خالق السموات والأرض ومن فيهما ـ سبحانه وتعالى علوا كبيرًا .. فالقسم الصحيح من الأقسام حذف في الآية؛ لدلالة المقام عليه، فدل التقسيم والسبر المذكوران على أن لهم خالقا هو الذي خلقهم، وهو الذي يستحق عليهم أن يعبدوه وحده، فكونهم خلقهم خالق تصديق، وكان التقسيم جزء الدليل عليه، ومن المعلوم أن الحصر والتقسيم إن كانا قطعيين كان السبر والتقسيم دليلاً قطعيًا، كالآية التي مثلنا بها، فدلالتها على أنه - تعالى - هو الخالق المستحق للعبادة وحده قطعية ؛ لأن الحصر والإبطال [فيها] قطعيان . واعلم أن الأصوليين إنما يستعملون هذا الدليل في إثبات العلة أو صف المحل، وإبطال غير الصالح للتعليل، فيتعين الباقي بحصر الصالح للتعليل . كقول الشافعي : علة تحريم الربا في البر إما الكيل وإما الطعم وإما الاقتيات والادخار مثلاً، ثم يستدل على بطلان وصف الكيل ووصف الاقتيات والادخار بأن ملء الكف من البر لا يجوز فيه الربا، مع أنه غير
The division is sound and fulfills its mentioned conditions, and its testing (sabr) is sound—which is examining its valid from its invalid, and its suitable from others—then the two mentioned things constitute an assertoric proof (dalil tasdiqi), and the division is part of it. And this proof, which is sabr wa-taqsim, is used by logicians for other purposes, and it is what we previously stated they call the disjunctive conditional (al-sharti al-munfasil). Know that we have expanded the discussion on this proof in our book 'Adwa' al-Bayan fi Idah al-Qur'an bi al-Qur'an' in Surah Maryam, regarding His statement, Exalted: 'Has he looked into the unseen or taken a covenant with the Most Merciful?' and we explained its intended meaning according to the usuliyyun, jadaliyyun, and mantiqiyyun, and clarified the difference between what each of them intends by it and what the other intends, and we mentioned four Qur'anic examples for it, and explained some of its historical, creedal, and literary effects. Therefore we will shorten the discussion on it here, and mention one example from the Qur'an, one from usul, and one from jadal. Among its examples in the Qur'an is His statement, Exalted: 'Or were they created from nothing, or are they the creators?' [Q. 52:35]. And we have stated that the division in this verse is rational, and the divisions are three because they are two rational, certain divisions. For we say: either they were created with no creator at all, or a creator created them—and this is a rational, certain division because it exhausts a thing and its negation. Then we divide the second division, which is our saying: 'or a creator created them,' and we say: that creator is not free from being themselves or not being themselves—and this is also a rational, certain division because it exhausts a thing and its negation. Thus the divisions in the two divisions become three: first, that they were created with no creator at all; second, that they created themselves; third, that a creator other than themselves created them. So this sound rational division is part of the assertoric proof, and its other part is the testing (sabr) of these three divisions and examining them, distinguishing the sound from the false. By the sound testing it becomes clear that the first two divisions—namely, that they were created without a creator or that they created themselves—are extremely false, as is not hidden, and that the third division—namely, that a creator created them—is sound, no doubt about it. And this creator who created them is the Creator of the heavens and the earth and what is in them—glorified and exalted be He, a great exaltation. The sound division from among the divisions was omitted in the verse because the context indicates it. So the mentioned division and testing indicate that they have a Creator who created them, and He is the one who deserves that they worship Him alone. Thus that a Creator created them is an assent (tasdiq), and the division was part of the proof for it. And it is known that if the enumeration (hasr) and division are certain, then the sabr wa-taqsim is a certain proof, like the verse we have exemplified. So its indication that He, Exalted, is the Creator deserving of worship alone is certain because the enumeration and nullification in it are certain. And know that the usuliyyun only use this proof to establish the 'illa (cause) of the attributes of the subject and to nullify what is unsuitable for ta'lil (legal reasoning), so the remaining is determined by the enumeration of what is suitable for ta'lil. Like the statement of al-Shafi'i: the cause of the prohibition of riba in wheat is either measurement by weight or by volume (al-kayl) or edibility (al-ta'm) or storable sustenance (al-iqtitat wa al-iddikhar) for example, then he argues for the nullity of the attribute of measurement and the attribute of storable sustenance by the fact that a handful of wheat does not permit riba in it, even though it is not
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage explains the logical method of sabr wa-taqsim (testing and division) as a type of assertoric proof (dalil tasdiqi). The author begins by stating that if a division fulfills its conditions and its testing (sabr) that distinguishes valid from invalid is sound, then the two together constitute a proof, with division as a part. This proof is also called the disjunctive conditional (sharti munfasil) by logicians. The author refers to an earlier expanded treatment in his work 'Adwa' al-Bayan' and now summarizes, giving three examples: one from the Qur'an, one from usul al-fiqh, and one from jadal. The Qur'anic example is Q. 52:35: 'Or were they created from nothing, or are they the creators?' The author analyzes this verse as containing two rational, certain divisions. First, humans are either created without a creator or by a creator. This is certain because it exhausts a thing and its negation. Second, if created by a creator, that creator is either themselves or another. This is also certain for the same reason. Thus three possibilities emerge: no creator, self-created, or created by another. The sabr (testing) then examines these: the first two are false (absurd), so the third is true—they are created by a Creator, who is Allah. The verse omits the third possibility because context indicates it. The author emphasizes that if the enumeration (hasr) and division are certain, the whole proof is certain. The verse's indication is certain because both the exhaustive enumeration and the nullification of false options are certain. The author then mentions how usuliyyun (legal theorists) use this method: they establish the 'illa (legal cause) by listing possible attributes of a subject (e.g., for riba in wheat: measurement by volume, edibility, storable sustenance), then test and eliminate those that do not hold in all cases. An example from Imam al-Shafi'i is given: he posits that the cause for riba in wheat is either measurement (al-kayl), edibility (al-ta'm), or storable sustenance (al-iqtitat wa al-iddikhar). Then he nullifies measurement and storable sustenance by pointing out that a handful of wheat (which is not precisely measured) still involves riba, so those attributes are not the cause, leaving edibility as the correct 'illa. This passage demonstrates the power of sabr wa-taqsim as a tool for certainty in both theology and jurisprudence.

Key Terms
علة (ʿilla): The effective cause or ratio legis in Islamic jurisprudence that links a ruling to its subject. [Note: Stable term in usul al-fiqh.]
تعليل (taʿlīl): The process of legal reasoning to identify the cause (ʿilla) of a ruling. [Note: Stable term.]
حصر (ḥaṣr): Exhaustive enumeration of all possibilities in a division, ensuring certainty if the division is dichotomous. [Note: Stable term; used in mantiq and usul.]

Sabr wa-taqsim in usul al-fiqh provides a method to pinpoint the legal cause by testing each candidate against counterexamples.

The Syllogism
intermediate

Constructing Objections as First-Figure Syllogisms
Author explains the standard method of objecting via syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a valid objection against a definition as a categorical syllogism in the first figure.

Core Idea
Objections are first-figure categorical syllogisms: minor = defect, major = rule, conclusion = verdict.

After learning the types of objections to definitions, you must now learn the standard method of presenting them. The author teaches that every objection can be formulated as a categorical syllogism (qiyās iqtirānī) in the first figure (al-shakl al-awwal). This ensures logical rigor and forces clarity in dialectical exchanges. The pattern is simple: state the defect as the minor premise, add a general rule as the major premise, and derive the conclusion about the definition being invalid or defective.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defect (wajh al-iʿtirāḍ)
Pinpoint the specific flaw in the definition, e.g., it is not preventing (ghayr māniʿ) or has an error in wording.
The defect becomes the minor premise.
2
Formulate the minor premise
Express the defect as a proposition: 'This definition has defect X.' This is the minor premise of the syllogism.
It should be a simple categorical statement.
3
Formulate the major premise
State a general rule: 'Every definition that has defect X is invalid (fāsid) or not good (ghayr ḥasan).' This is the major premise.
The major premise must use the same defect term as the minor.
4
Derive the conclusion via first figure
From the minor and major, the conclusion 'This definition is invalid/not good' follows automatically because the middle term (the defect) is subject in the major and predicate in the minor (first figure).
The first figure ensures validity if both premises are true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا عرفت أوجه الاعتراض على التعريف، فلا يخفى عليك أن الطريقة المعهودة في ذلك هي أن تجعل وجه الاعتراض مقدمة صغرى من دليل المعترض، وهو قياس اقتراني من الشكل الأول، وتضم إليها كبرى صورتها مثلاً : وكل تعريف كان كذلك فهو فاسد، أو فهو غير حسن).
كقولك : هذا التعريف الحقيقي غير مانع، وكل تعريف حقيقي كان كذلك فهو فاسد ينتج : هذا التعريف الحقيقي فاسد، وهو المراد، وهكذا.
وكقولك : هذا التعريف في بعض ألفاظه غلط، وكل تعريف كان كذلك فهو غير حسن ينتج من الشكل الأول: هذا التعريف غير حسن، وهكذا.
And if you have known the aspects of objection to definition, it is not hidden from you that the customary method in that is that you make the aspect of objection a minor premise of the evidence of the objector, and it is a categorical syllogism (qiyās iqtirānī) in the first figure (al-shakl al-awwal), and you join to it a major whose form is e.g.: 'And every definition that is such is invalid (fāsid), or it is not good (ghayr ḥasan).' Such as your saying: 'This real definition is not preventing (māniʻ), and every real definition that is such is invalid,' resulting: 'This real definition is invalid,' which is the intended. And so on. And such as your saying: 'This definition has an error in some of its wording, and every definition that is such is not good,' resulting from the first figure: 'This definition is not good.' And so on.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage explains the method of raising an objection against a definition by presenting it as a categorical syllogism in the first figure. The author states that once you understand the various types of objections (covered in the previous passage), you should know that the standard procedure is to make the specific defect (wajh al-i'tirad) the minor premise (muqaddima sughra) of the objector's proof (dalil al-mu'tarid). This proof is a categorical syllogism (qiyas iqtirani) in the first figure (al-shakl al-awwal). You then add a major premise (kubra) that states a general rule: for example, 'Every definition that is such (i.e., has that defect) is invalid (fasid)' or 'is not good (ghayr hasan).' The author gives two concrete examples. First: 'This real definition is not preventing (mani'), and every real definition that is not preventing is invalid,' which yields the conclusion 'This real definition is invalid.' Second: 'This definition has an error in some of its wording, and every definition that has such an error is not good,' yielding 'This definition is not good.' In both cases, the conclusion follows from the first figure (which has the middle term as subject of major and predicate of minor). This method standardizes objections, ensuring logical rigor in dialectic. The author emphasizes that the example pattern can be extended to any defect.

Key Terms
طَرِيقَة مَعْهُودَة
قِيَاس اِقْتِرَانِي
الشَّكْل الأَوَّل

The Syllogism

The Natural Syllogism as Self-Evident Reasoning
Author defines the natural syllogism as a type of obvious self-evident proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a natural syllogism in an Islamic text and reconstruct its first-figure categorical form.

Core Idea
The natural syllogism is self-evident: once its terms are conceived, the conclusion follows necessarily.

This passage introduces the concept of the natural syllogism (القياس الطبيعي) as an innate, self-evident reasoning process. Unlike formal syllogisms that require conscious arrangement, the natural syllogism operates automatically once its terms are understood. The author illustrates this with the judgment that four is even, showing how the mind necessarily draws the conclusion and then formalizes it as a first-figure categorical syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition
Locate the phrase 'أنه بمعنى مركوز في طبيعة الإنسان' — the natural syllogism is ingrained in human nature and cannot be absent from the mind. Note that this is not a learned inference but an immediate, necessary judgment.
2
Analyze the inferential necessity
The author says the conclusion follows necessarily (للزوم إدراكه لإدراك طرفي القضية). Reflect: why is the conclusion unavoidable once you conceive 'four' and 'evenness'? The mind automatically breaks down the meaning of 'four' as a number divisible into two equal parts.
This is a form of conceptual analysis (tahlil al-mafhum).
3
Extract the syllogistic form
Write the syllogism as given: 'الأربعة عدد منقسم إلى متساويين وكل عدد كان كذلك فهو زوج ينتج من الشكل الأول: الأربعة زوج.' Identify the minor premise (Four is a number divisible into two equal parts), the major premise (Every number divisible into two equal parts is even), and the conclusion (Four is even).
The minor term is 'four', the major term is 'even', and the middle term is 'number divisible into two equal parts'.
4
Verify the figure and mood
In this syllogism, the middle term is the subject of the major premise and the predicate of the minor premise. This is the first figure. The mood is AAA because all three propositions are universal affirmatives (treating 'four' as a singular universal). This mood is valid in the first figure.
Formalization: (Barbara) All M are P, All S are M, therefore All S are P.
5
Connect to 'badihi jali fitri'
The author calls this conclusion 'البديهي الجلي الفطري' — the innate obvious self-evident. Understand that the natural syllogism is the underlying reasoning that makes such propositions self-evident. It is not a formal exercise but the very structure of immediate knowledge.
The author refers back to an earlier discussion on types of signification (الدلالة) regarding the necessary concomitant (اللازم).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنه بمعنى مركوز في طبيعة الإنسان لا يمكن أن يغيب عن ذهنه؛ للزوم إدراكه لإدراك طرفي القضية، كالحكم على الأربعة بأنها زوج؛ لأنك إذا تصورت معنى الأربعة والزوجية لزم عقلاً أن تدرك أنها عدد منقسم إلى متساويين، وتدرك أن كل عدد منقسم إلى متساويين فهو زوج. وصورة القياس الطبيعي المذكور أن تقول: الأربعة عدد منقسم إلى متساويين وكل عدد كان كذلك فهو زوج ينتج من الشكل الأول: الأربعة زوج. وهذا القياس لا يغيب عن ذهنك، وهذه النتيجة هي ما يسمى بالبديهي الجلي الفطري، وقد قدمنا ما يزيد هذا إيضاحا في الكلام على أنواع الدلالة في مبحث اللازم في الذهن والخارج معا (۱).
It is in the sense of being ingrained in human nature, unable to be absent from the mind, because its perception necessarily follows from perceiving the two terms of the proposition, like the judgment on four that it is even; for when you conceive the meaning of four and evenness, the mind necessarily understands that it is a number divisible into two equal parts, and you understand that every number divisible into two equal parts is even. The form of the aforementioned natural syllogism is to say: Four is a number divisible into two equal parts, and every number that is such is even; this yields from the first figure: Four is even. This syllogism does not leave your mind, and this result is what is called the innate obvious self-evident. We have already presented what increases its clarification in the discussion on types of signification in the topic of the necessary concomitant together in the mind and external world (1).
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage defines the natural syllogism as a type of obvious self-evident proposition (badihi jali). The author explains that it is ingrained in human nature and cannot be absent from the mind, because once you conceive the two terms of the proposition, the judgment necessarily follows. He gives the example of judging that four is even: if you conceive 'four' and 'evenness', the mind necessarily understands that four is a number divisible into two equal parts, and that every such number is even. Then he presents the explicit syllogistic form: 'Four is a number divisible into two equal parts; every number that is such is even; therefore, four is even' in the first figure. This syllogism is always present in the mind, and its conclusion is called innate obvious self-evident (badihi jali fitri). He notes that this was earlier clarified in the discussion on types of signification regarding the necessary concomitant (lazim) both in the mind and externally.

القياس الطبيعي
البديهي الجلي
اللازم

A) Because it is memorized from childhood.
B) Because once you conceive 'four' and 'evenness', the conclusion follows necessarily.
C) Because it is taught by logicians.
D) Because it is a revealed truth.

Key Takeaway
The natural syllogism is an innate, self-evident reasoning process where the conclusion follows necessarily from the mere conception of the terms, exemplifying the first figure of categorical syllogisms.

Hadsiyat: Intuitive Judgments and the Instantaneous Syllogism
Author explains hadsiyat as a type of badihi khafi with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify hadsiyat as a type of badihi khafi and explicate the role of the middle term in producing instantaneous conclusions.

Core Idea
Intuitive judgments depend on a middle term recognized instantly, not on the absence of logic.

In this lesson, we explore the concept of hadsiyat (intuitive judgments) as defined by classical logicians. The author distinguishes hads from discursive reasoning (fikr) by emphasizing the instantaneous nature of the mental transition. We will see that even these 'self-evident' judgments rely on a middle term, though its recognition is immediate. This connects to how we handle axiomatic starting points in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of hads
Find where the author says the transition is instantaneous (دفعي لا تدريجي). Note the contrast with fikr.
2
Identify the key claim about badihi
The author says hadsiyat are badihiyat, not nazariyat, meaning they require no prior proof.
3
Extract the syllogism example
Write out the categorical syllogism: major premise, minor premise, conclusion. Identify the middle term (cause).
4
Understand the role of the middle term
The author says reflection on the middle term suffices. Explain how recognition of the causal relation yields the conclusion instantly.
5
Connect to the alternative definition
How does the second definition (based on sensed objects) align with the first? Both emphasize lack of need for prolonged reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالانتقال في الحدس دفعي لا تدريجي، عكس الفكر. انتهى محل الغرض منه .
ومعنى كلامه أن الحدسيات لا يُحتاج فيها إلى العلم بدليلها ثم الانتقالِ من علم الدليل إلى علم المدلول الذي هو الحدسيات؛ لأنها من البديهيات لا من النظريات، بل يكفي في إدراكها التأمل في الطرف المتكرر الذي هو الحد الأوسط كما تقدم إيضاحه .
فالحد الأوسط في المثال المتقدم كون ارتفاع ماء الأنهار سببًا في ارتفاع ماء الآبار، لأنك تقول : ارتفاع ماء الأنهار سبب في ارتفاع ماء الآبار، وكل سبب في شيء يوجد معه مسببه، ينتج من الشكل الأول : ارتفاع ماء الأنهار يوجد معه مسبّبه، أي وهو ارتفاع ماء الآبار، وهو المطلوب، فإذا التفت إلى هذا المطلوب بالحد الوسط الذي هو كون هذا سببًا هذا حصل لك المطلوب دفعة من غير تدريج، وهو معنى كون الانتقال فيه دفعيًا لا تدريجيا، والله - تعالى - أعلم .
وقال بعضهم : هو كل قضية يحكم العقل بثبوت محمولها لموضوعها بناءً على محسوسات أخرى لا يحتاج العقل في ترتب هذه عليها إلى نظر واستدلال . وهو راجع لما ذكرنا .
The transition in intuition (hads) is instantaneous, not gradual, unlike discursive thinking (fikr). End of the relevant part. And the meaning of his statement is that intuitive judgments (hadsiyat) do not require knowledge of their proof and then transition from knowledge of the proof to knowledge of the proven, because they are from self-evident matters (badihiyat) not theoretical ones; rather, for their apprehension, reflection on the repeated term which is the middle term is sufficient, as previously explained. So the middle term in the previous example is that rising water of rivers is a cause for rising water of wells, because you say: Rising water of rivers is a cause for rising water of wells, and every cause of something exists with its effect, yields from the first figure: Rising water of rivers exists with its effect, i.e., rising water of wells, which is the desired conclusion. So when you attend to this desired conclusion through the middle term which is that this is a cause, the desired conclusion occurs to you instantly without gradation, and that is the meaning of the transition being instantaneous, not gradual. And Allah knows best. And some said: It is every proposition whose subject's possession of its predicate the mind judges based on other sensed things (mahsusat) without the mind needing for the ordering of these upon those any consideration (nazar) or inference (istidlal). And that returns to what we mentioned.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage defines hadsiyat (intuitive judgments) as a type of self-evident (badihi) yet hidden (khafi) knowledge. The author explains that the mental transition in hads is instantaneous, unlike gradual discursive reasoning (fikr). He elaborates: hadsiyat do not require prior knowledge of a proof; rather, one simply reflects on the middle term (al-hadd al-awsat) which, once recognized, yields the conclusion instantly. The example given is: rising river water causes rising well water. The syllogism is: Rising river water is a cause for rising well water; every cause exists with its effect; therefore, rising river water exists with its effect, i.e., rising well water. The middle term is the causal relation. When one becomes aware of this relation, the conclusion is immediate. Finally, he cites another definition: a hadsi proposition is one whose truth is judged based on other sensed data without need for reasoning. This aligns with the earlier explanation.

الحد الأوسط
الدليل

Hadsiyat are self-evident yet rely on a middle term; their immediacy stems from instantaneous recognition, not absence of logical structure.

The Syllogism
intermediate

Man' in Dialectical Syllogisms: Examples and Structure
Author defines man' and gives five examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which premise is being prevented (minor or major) in both categorical and conditional syllogisms, and distinguish between explicit prevention and a request for a reminder (tanbih).

Core Idea
Man' is the opponent's surgical rejection of a specific premise in a proof.

In munazara, when a proponent presents a syllogistic proof, the opponent may respond with 'I prevent' (amna‘u) a particular premise. This move forces the proponent to either defend the premise or refine the argument. The passage gives five concrete examples from Islamic legal and theological reasoning, showing how man' targets the minor or major premise in both categorical (iqtirani) and conditional (istithna'i) syllogisms. It also notes a nuance: for hidden self-evident premises, the opponent asks for a reminder (tanbih) rather than a new proof.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the proponent's syllogistic structure
Identify the claim (conclusion) and the two premises (minor and major) in the proponent's argument. In categorical syllogisms, the minor contains the subject of the conclusion; the major contains the predicate. In conditional syllogisms (conjunctive or disjunctive), the major is the conditional premise and the minor is the exception (istithna').
The passage recaps these distinctions before giving examples.
2
Locate the opponent's 'I prevent' statement
The opponent explicitly says 'I prevent' (amna‘u) followed by specifying which premise is challenged: the minor (sughra) or the major (kubra) of the proof. The prevention may be direct or implicit as a demand for evidence (dahl).
Some scholars consider any demand for evidence as implicit prevention.
3
Classify the prevention type by syllogism kind
Note whether the argument is categorical or conditional. In categorical examples (apples, horses, donkeys), prevention targets the categorical minor or major. In conditional examples (human, maize), prevention targets the conditional major (the shartiyya) or the minor (the istithna').
Example 3 prevents the conditional premise itself; Example 4 prevents the exception.
4
Distinguish between man' and tanbih
The author notes a refinement: if the premise is a hidden self-evident (khafiyy badihi) truth, the opponent does not demand a new proof but only a reminder (tanbih) to remove the hiddenness. This is still considered a form of man' in some terminologies.
This nuance prevents the definition of man' from being too narrow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد قدمنا في المقدمة المنطقية أن المقدمة الصغرى في القياس الاقتراني هي المشتملة على الحد الأصغر، وهو موضوع النتيجة دائما، وأن المقدمة الكبرى فيه هي المشتملة على الحد الأكبر، وهو محمول النتيجة دائمًا، وأن المقدمة الكبرى في الاستثنائى هي الشرطية، متصلةً كانت أو منفصلة، وأن الصغرى فيه كما نقله ابن عرفة عن الفارابي، وقد أوضحنا كل ذلك فيما تقدم
الاستثنائية،
وكثير من أهل هذا الفن يعبرون عن المنع بالمطالبة بالدليل، والمطالبة به في الحقيقة كأنها منع ضمني، وعلى كل حال فهي عندهم منع، وبعضهم يعترض على المنع المذكور بالمطالبة بالدليل؛ لأنه لا يشمل البديهي الخفي؛ لأن صاحبه لا يطالب بدليل، وإنما يطالب بتنبيه يزيل الخفاء كما عرفت مما تقدم .
ومثال منع المقدمة الصغرى في هذه الطريق من طرق المناظرة في الاقتراني ما لو قال : التفاح ربوي ثم أقام الدليل على هذا التصديق فقال : كل تفاح مكيل وكل مكيل ربوي، ينتج من الشكل الأول : التفاح ربوي .
فيقول خصمه : أمنع صُغرى دليلك هذا، وهي قولك : كل تفاح مكيل .
ومثال منع الكبرى أن يقول : الخيل السائمة تجب فيها الزكاة، ثم يقيم الدليل على ذلك فيقول : الخيل السائمة مال، وكل مال تجب فيه الزكاة، ينتج من الشكل الأول : الخيل السائمة تجب فيها الزكاة. فيقول خصمه : أمنعُ كُبرى دليلك هذا، وهي قولك: وكل مال تجب فيه الزكاة.
ومثاله في الاستثنائي أن يريد إثبات أن هذا إنسان فيقول : لو كان هذا حيوانا لكان إنسانًا، لكنه حيوان ينتج فهو إنسان . فيقول خصمه : أمنعُ المقدمة الكبرى من دليلك هذا، وهي الشرطية، أعني قولك : لو كان هذا حيوانًا لكان إنسانًا .
ومثال منع الصغرى في القياس الاستثنائي أن يريد إثبات أن الذُرَة ليست بربوية، فيقول : لو كانت الذرة ربوية لكانت مكيلة، لكنها غيرُ مكيلة، ينتج : فهي غير ربوية .
فيقول خصمه : أمنعُ صُغرى دليلك هذا، وهي قولك : لكنها غيرُ مكيلة .
ومثاله في التنبيه أن يقول صاحب التصديق : حرمة الحمر الأهلية قطعية . ثم يقيم الدليل على ذلك فيقول : حرمتها أخبر بها عن النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام عدد يستحيل تواطؤهم على الكذب، وكل حرمة أخبر بها عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم عدد يستحيل تواطؤهم على الكذب فهي قطعية، ينتج من الشكل الأول : حرمة الحمر الأهلية قطعية .
فيقول خصمه : أمنعُ صغرى دليلك هذا، وهي قولك: إن حرمتها أخبر بها عدد يستحيل تواطؤهم على الكذب .
We have already presented in the logical introduction that the minor premise in the categorical syllogism is the one containing the minor term, which is always the subject of the conclusion, and that the major premise in it is the one containing the major term, which is always the predicate of the conclusion, and that the major premise in the conditional [syllogism] is the conditional, whether conjunctive or disjunctive, and that the minor in it is as transmitted by Ibn 'Arafa from al-Farabi, and we have clarified all that in what preceded.

The conditional [syllogism].

And many practitioners of this art express prevention (al-man') as a demand for evidence, and the demand for it is in reality an implicit prevention, and in any case, it is prevention according to them. And some object to the mentioned prevention by demanding evidence because it does not include the hidden self-evident [premise]; because its possessor is not asked for evidence, but rather is asked for a reminder that removes the hiddenness, as you have understood from what preceded.

An example of preventing the minor premise in this way from the ways of disputation in the categorical [syllogism] is if one says: "Apples are ribawi," then establishes the proof for this assertion by saying: "Every apple is measurable (makil), and every measurable is ribawi," which yields from the first figure: "Apples are ribawi." So his opponent says: "I prevent the minor of your proof, which is your statement: 'Every apple is measurable.'"

An example of preventing the major [premise] is that one says: "Horses pasturing freely (al-sa'ima) require zakat," then establishes the proof for that by saying: "Horses pasturing freely are property (mal), and every property requires zakat," which yields from the first figure: "Horses pasturing freely require zakat." So his opponent says: "I prevent the major of your proof, which is your statement: 'And every property requires zakat.'"

Its example in the conditional [syllogism] is if one wants to prove that this is a human and says: "If this were an animal, it would be a human, but it is an animal," which yields: "It is a human." So his opponent says: "I prevent the major premise of your proof, which is the conditional, namely your statement: 'If this were an animal, it would be a human.'"

An example of preventing the minor in the conditional syllogism is if one wants to prove that maize (al-dhura) is not ribawi and says: "If maize were ribawi, it would be measurable, but it is not measurable," which yields: "It is not ribawi." So his opponent says: "I prevent the minor of your proof, which is your statement: 'But it is not measurable.'"

Its example in reminder (al-tanbih) is if the proponent of the assertion says: "The prohibition of domestic donkeys (al-humur al-ahliyya) is certain (qat'iyya)." Then he establishes the proof for that by saying: "Its prohibition was reported about the Prophet (peace be upon him) by a number [of transmitters] whose collusion on lying is impossible, and every prohibition reported about the Prophet (peace be upon him) by a number whose collusion on lying is impossible is certain," which yields from the first figure: "The prohibition of domestic donkeys is certain." So his opponent says: "I prevent the minor of your proof, which is your statement: 'Its prohibition was reported by a number whose collusion on lying is impossible.'"
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exposition defining and illustrating the concept of 'man'' (prevention) in the context of munazara (disputation). The author begins by recapitulating the structure of categorical and conditional syllogisms, establishing that the minor premise contains the subject of the conclusion and the major contains the predicate. For conditional syllogisms, the major is the conditional premise (conjunctive or disjunctive), and the minor is the excluded premise. He then defines man' as the opponent's demand for evidence, which functions as an implicit prevention. A refinement is noted: some object that this definition excludes the hidden self-evident premise, which requires only a reminder (tanbih) rather than a full proof. The core of the passage provides five concrete examples:

1. **Preventing the minor in a categorical syllogism (riba example):** The proponent claims apples are ribawi and offers a first-figure syllogism: every apple is measurable, every measurable is ribawi, therefore apples are ribawi. The opponent prevents the minor by denying that all apples are measurable.
2. **Preventing the major in a categorical syllogism (zakat example):** The proponent claims pasturing horses require zakat, arguing: every pasturing horse is property, every property requires zakat. The opponent prevents the major by denying that all property requires zakat (since some property is exempt).
3. **Preventing the major in a conditional syllogism (human example):** To prove something is human, the proponent uses a conditional: if it were an animal it would be human; it is an animal, therefore it is human. The opponent prevents the conditional premise (the major) itself.
4. **Preventing the minor in a conditional syllogism (maize example):** To prove maize is not ribawi, the proponent uses: if maize were ribawi it would be measurable; but it is not measurable, therefore not ribawi. The opponent prevents the minor (the exception) by denying that maize is not measurable.
5. **Preventing the minor in a categorical syllogism with a tanbih (donkey example):** The proponent asserts the prohibition of domestic donkeys is certain, offering a proof: the prohibition was reported via tawatur (mass transmission) which yields certainty, and every such prohibition is certain. The opponent prevents the minor by disputing that the prohibition was reported via tawatur.

These examples illustrate how man' functions as a key dialectical move to challenge the premises of an opponent's proof, forcing the proponent either to defend the premise or refine the argument.

Key Terms
المنع
المقدمة الصغرى
المقدمة الكبرى
القياس الاقتراني
القياس الاستثنائي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Man' is a dialectical move that targets a specific premise in a syllogistic proof, forcing the proponent to defend it or provide a reminder.

Man' al-Muqaddima: Denying a Premise in Debate
Author illustrates man' al-muqaddima with three examples in categorical and conditional syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which premise (major or minor) is being prevented in both categorical and conditional syllogisms, and apply man' as a dialectical objection.

Core Idea
Man' al-muqaddima: block the premise, block the conclusion.

In dialectical debate (jadal), one of the most incisive moves is to simply deny a premise of your opponent's argument. This is called man' al-muqaddima. The author illustrates it with three examples: one categorical syllogism about zakat on horses, and two conditional syllogisms about humanity and usurious corn. You will learn to spot exactly which premise is under attack and understand the strategic effect of this objection.

Guided Path
1
Restate the proponent's argument
For each example, write out the full syllogism clearly: premises and conclusion. For the first: 'Free-grazing horses are property (sughra). Every property is zakatable (kubra). Therefore free-grazing horses are zakatable.'
2
Locate the opponent's 'I prevent' (amna'u) statement
Find where the opponent says 'amna'u' and note whether he says 'kubra' (major) or 'sughra' (minor). In the first, he prevents the kubra; in the second, the kubra; in the third, the sughra.
3
Classify the syllogism type
Determine whether the syllogism is categorical (qiyas iqtirani) or conditional (qiyas istithna'i). The first is categorical; the next two are conditional.
In conditional syllogisms, the major is the conditional statement itself, and the minor is the categorical premise.
4
Analyze the effect of the objection
If the opponent blocks a premise, the conclusion does not logically follow. The proponent must now defend the blocked premise or modify his argument. Notice that the objection is a simple denial—no counter-proof is required at this stage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقيم الدليل على ذلك فيقول : الخيل السائمة مال، وكل مال تجب فيه الزكاة، ينتج من الشكل الأول : الخيل السائمة تجب فيها الزكاة. فيقول خصمه : أمنعُ كُبرى دليلك هذا، وهي قولك: وكل مال تجب فيه الزكاة. ومثاله في الاستثنائي أن يريد إثبات أن هذا إنسان فيقول : لو كان هذا حيوانا لكان إنسانًا، لكنه حيوان ينتج فهو إنسان . فيقول خصمه : أمنعُ المقدمة الكبرى من دليلك هذا، وهي الشرطية، أعني قولك : لو كان هذا حيوانًا لكان إنسانًا . ومثال منع الصغرى في القياس الاستثنائي أن يريد إثبات أن الذُرَة ليست بربوية، فيقول : لو كانت الذرة ربوية لكانت مكيلة، لكنها غيرُ مكيلة، ينتج : فهي غير ربوية . فيقول خصمه : أمنعُ صُغرى دليلك هذا، وهي قولك : لكنها غيرُ مكيلة .
He establishes the proof on that by saying: The free-grazing horse is property, and every property on which zakat is obligatory, (thus) from the first figure results: The free-grazing horse has zakat obligatory on it. So his opponent says: I prevent the major premise of your proof, which is your statement: 'and every property on which zakat is obligatory.' And its example in the conditional (syllogism) is that he wants to prove that this is a human, so he says: If this were an animal it would be a human, but it is an animal, so it results that it is a human. So his opponent says: I prevent the major premise of your proof, which is the conditional, I mean your statement: 'If this were an animal it would be a human.' And an example of preventing the minor premise in the conditional syllogism is that he wants to prove that corn is not usurious, so he says: If corn were usurious it would be measurable by volume, but it is not measurable by volume, so it results: So it is not usurious. So his opponent says: I prevent the minor premise of your proof, which is your statement: 'but it is not measurable by volume.'
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage provides three concrete examples of the dialectical move known as 'man' al-muqaddima' (preventing/premise denial) in different types of syllogisms. The first example is in a categorical syllogism (qiyas iqtirani) of the first figure. The proponent argues: 'Free-grazing horses are property, and every property is liable for zakat, therefore free-grazing horses are liable for zakat.' The opponent responds by denying the major premise: 'I prevent (your statement) that every property is liable for zakat.' This is a standard objection; the opponent does not accept the universal major as true. The second example is in a conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i) where the proponent wants to prove that something is a human by using the conditional: 'If this were an animal it would be a human.' The opponent prevents the major (the conditional statement itself). The third example is again a conditional syllogism, but this time the opponent denies the minor premise (the categorical assumption that corn is not measurable by volume). These examples show that man' can target either the major or the minor premise in both categorical and conditional syllogisms. The context is the art of debate (jadal); the objector's aim is to block the conclusion by casting doubt on a specific premise.

منع
كبرى
صغرى
استثنائي

Key Takeaway
Man' al-muqaddima is a dialectical tool to block a syllogism by denying a premise, forcing the proponent to defend it.
