Invalidity of First-Figure Syllogism with Negative Minor Premise
Author exposes a common fallacy in using a negative minor premise in first-figure syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a first-figure categorical syllogism has an invalid mood due to a negative minor premise and explain why the conclusion's truth does not validate the form.

Core Idea
A true conclusion does not make an invalid syllogism valid.

This cautionary note highlights a common logical error among practitioners: using a negative minor premise in the first figure. Even when the conclusion happens to be true in reality, the syllogistic form is invalid because it violates the fundamental condition that the minor premise must be affirmative. Understanding this fallacy sharpens your ability to distinguish between valid reasoning and mere coincidence in argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and conclusion
Locate the major premise, minor premise, and conclusion in the example argument: 'This figure is not human; everything not human is not laughing; therefore this figure is not laughing.' The minor premise is 'This figure is not human'.
2
Determine the figure
Check the position of the middle term. The middle term 'not human' is subject in the major premise and predicate in the minor premise, which is the first figure.
3
Verify the condition for first-figure validity
Recall that for a first-figure syllogism to be valid, the minor premise must be affirmative. Here the minor premise is negative, violating the shart al-intaj.
4
Assess why the conclusion is still true
The conclusion happens to be true because in reality, non-human figures do not laugh. But this is due to 'khusus al-madda' (particular material), not the logical form. The syllogism does not necessitate the conclusion.
5
Recognize the fallacy pattern
Be alert: whenever you see a first-figure syllogism with a negative minor, the reasoning is invalid regardless of the truth of the conclusion. This is a common sophistical error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التنبيه الثاني : اعلم أنه يجري على ألسنة أهل هذا الفن كثيرًا نحو هذه العبارات : كقولهم هذا الشبح ليس بضاحك) ويقيمون الدليل على هذا التصديق السلبي بقولهم لأنه ليس بإنسان، وكل ماليس بإنسان ليس بضاحك، فالشبح ليس بضاحك)، وهذا الدليل وإن أنتج المقصود، الذي هو كون ذلك الشبح ليس بضاحك، فإنما أنتج ذلك لخصوص المادة، والدليل في حد ذاته باطل؛ لأنه قياس اقتراني من الشكل الأول، ومعلوم أنه يُشترط لإنتاجه أن تكون صغراه موجبة، وهذا الدليل المذكور صغراه سالبة، وهي قولهم : لأنه ليس بإنسان، ولا يجوز الاستدلال بالشكل الأول في حال كون صغراه سالبة لاختلال شرط الإنتاج كما تقدم إيضاحه، وإن أنتج المقصود في بعض الحالات لخصوص المادة، فتنبه لذلك، والخطب سهل ؛ لأن المثال يغتفر فيه ما لا يغتفر في غيره كما تقدم. واعلم أن السند قد يقسم تقسيما آخر باعتبار آخر، فهو ينقسم باعتبار نسبته إلى نقيض الدعوى الممنوعة في نفس الأمر لا بالنظر إلى اعتقاد المانع إلى ستة أقسام، وسيأتي تفصيلها بأمثلتها .
The second caution: Know that on the tongues of the people of this art, many expressions like these occur: such as their saying 'This figure is not laughing,' and they establish proof for this negative assent by saying: 'Because it is not human, and everything that is not human is not laughing, therefore the figure is not laughing.' And this proof, although it yields the intended result, which is that the figure is not laughing, it yields that only due to the particular material, and the proof in itself is invalid because it is a categorical syllogism of the first figure, and it is well-known that for its production it is required that its minor premise be affirmative, and this aforementioned proof has a negative minor premise, which is their saying 'because it is not human.' And it is not permissible to argue by the first figure when its minor premise is negative due to the violation of the condition of production, as was clarified previously, even if it produces the intended result in some cases due to the particular material. So pay attention to that. And the matter is easy, because the example is excused for what is not excused in other cases, as previously. And know that the evidence (sanad) may be divided by another division according to another consideration: it is divided according to its relation to the opposite of the claim that is prevented in reality, not with consideration of the belief of the preventer, into six parts, and their details with examples will come later.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is a critical note identifying a common logical fallacy. The author warns that practitioners often use an invalid syllogistic form: they argue that a particular figure (shabah) is not laughing because it is not human, and everything not human is not laughing. This is a first-figure categorical syllogism, but the minor premise ('it is not human') is negative. The condition for a valid first-figure syllogism is that the minor premise must be affirmative. Therefore, the reasoning is invalid (the proof in itself is false), even though the conclusion ('the figure is not laughing') happens to be true in this specific case because of the particular material (maadda) — in reality, non-humans generally do not laugh. The author notes that this specific example is excusable as an illustrative example, but in principle, one must not use such invalid reasoning. Finally, the author transitions to mention that evidence (sanad) can be divided into six types based on its relation to the opposite of the claim, but details are postponed.

Key Terms
الشكل الأول (al-shakl al-awwal): The first figure of the categorical syllogism, where the middle term is subject in the major premise and predicate in the minor premise. [Note: No significant shift; standard in logic.]
صغراه سالبة (sughra saliba): A negative minor premise; in the first figure, the minor premise must be affirmative for validity. [Note: No significant shift.]
شرط الإنتاج (shart al-intaj): The condition for the syllogism to produce a necessary conclusion; for the first figure, the minor must be affirmative and the major universal. [Note: No significant shift.]
خصوص المادة (khusus al-madda): The specific content or subject matter; here, the fact that non-humans are indeed not laughing makes the conclusion true despite the invalid form. [Note: No significant shift.]

Key Takeaway
A first-figure categorical syllogism is valid only if its minor premise is affirmative; a negative minor premise invalidates the argument, regardless of the truth of the conclusion.

Proving an Equivalent Claim in Dialectic
Author illustrates second function by a syllogism with an impossible minor premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a categorical syllogism used in a dialectical context to identify an impossible premise and evaluate the logical equivalence of the conclusion to a denied claim.

Core Idea
An impossible premise can still yield a conclusion logically equivalent to the desired claim.

In this passage, the author demonstrates a dialectical technique: when a claim is denied, you can prove an equivalent claim instead. He provides a detailed example using a categorical syllogism about atoms and pre-eternity. We will dissect this syllogism, see why its minor premise is impossible, and understand how its conclusion is equivalent to 'the world is originated.'

Guided Path
1
Identify the Syllogism's Components
Locate the minor premise (al-ṣughrā), major premise (al-kubrā), and conclusion in the Arabic text. The minor premise is 'Every atom of the world assumed not preceded by non-existence is pre-eternal.' The major premise is 'Nothing pre-eternal in existence is non-existent.' The conclusion is 'No such atom is non-existent.'
2
Assess the Minor Premise's Possibility
The author declares the minor premise impossible (mustaḥīla) because no atom exists that has not been preceded by non-existence; every atom is temporally originated. Understand why: in kalam, atoms are created and perish.
3
Understand the Conclusion's Falsity
The conclusion (no atom of that kind is non-existent) is false because atoms do become non-existent. The falsity stems from the impossible minor premise, not from invalid logic.
4
Trace the Equivalence to the Denied Claim
The intended denied claim is 'The world is originated.' The author argues that the conclusion 'No such atom is non-existent' is equivalent to that claim because if the minor premise were true, the conclusion would directly imply the world is not originated, but its contradictory (every atom is preceded by non-existence) is true and equivalent to 'the world is originated.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومثال إنتاج الدليل ما يساوي الدعوى الممنوعة أن يقول : كل ذرة من العالم فُرض أنها لم تُسبق بعدم فهي أزلية، ولا شيء من الأزلي الوجودي بمنعدم، ينتج من الشكل الأول : لا شيء من ذرات العالم جميع المفروض أنها لم تسبق بعدم بمنعدمة، وهذه النتيجة كاذبة ؛ لأن ذرات العالم تنعدم شيئًا فشيئًا كما هو مشاهد، وإنما كذبت النتيجة لكون الصغرى مستحيلة، وهي قوله : (كل ذرة من العالم فُرض أنها لم تُسبق بعدم فهي أزلية) ؛ لاستحالة وجود ذرة منه لم تُسبق بعدم حتى تكون أزلية، ومن المعلوم أن الأزلي الوجودي لا يُعقل انعدامه ؛ إذ لو كان انعدامه جائزا لكان محتاجًا في وجوده الأول إلى مخصص يخصص وجوده، ويرجحه على عدمه المساويه عقلاً أزلاً، فيكون حادثا لترجيح المخصص المذكور وجودَه على عدمه، والفرض أنه أزلي، واستحالة الصغرى التي هي كل ذرة من العالم فُرض أنها لم تُسبق بعدم فهي أزلية يلزمها صحة نقيضها، وهي (كل ذرة من ذرات العالم مسبوقة بعدم) ؛ لتساوي ذراته لذاتها (٢)، فهذه النتيجة مثال لإنتاج الدليل مساوي الدعوى الممنوعة ؛ لأنها مساوية لقولك : العالم حادث
An example of producing a proof that is equal to the denied claim: he says: 'Every atom of the world that is assumed to not have been preceded by non-existence is pre-eternal, and nothing that is pre-eternal in existence is non-existent.' This yields from the first figure: 'None of the atoms of the world that are all assumed not to have been preceded by non-existence is non-existent.' And this conclusion is false, because the atoms of the world become non-existent little by little as is observed. The conclusion is false only because the minor premise is impossible, namely his statement: 'Every atom of the world that is assumed to not have been preceded by non-existence is pre-eternal' – because it is impossible for an atom of it to exist that has not been preceded by non-existence, such that it would be pre-eternal. And it is known that that which is pre-eternal in existence is inconceivable for it to become non-existent; for if its non-existence were possible, it would need in its first existence a particularizer (mukhassis) to specify its existence and give it preponderance over its non-existence that is equal to it rationally from pre-eternity, so it would be originated due to the aforementioned particularizer giving preponderance to its existence over its non-existence, while the assumption is that it is pre-eternal. And the impossibility of the minor premise, which is 'Every atom of the world that is assumed to not have been preceded by non-existence is pre-eternal', necessitates the truth of its contradictory, which is 'Every atom of the world's atoms is preceded by non-existence' – due to the equality of its atoms in themselves. So this conclusion is an example of producing a proof equal to the denied claim, because it is equal to your statement: 'The world is originated.'
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage expands on the second function of answering an objection: proving a claim equivalent (مساوي) to the denied claim. The author gives a detailed example from kalam about atoms. The prover tries to prove that no atom is non-existent, which is equivalent to saying the world is originated (because if every atom is non-existent at some point, the world is originated). The syllogism is: 'Every atom (not preceded by non-existence) is pre-eternal; nothing pre-eternal is non-existent; therefore no such atom is non-existent.' However, the conclusion is false because atoms do become non-existent. The falsity stems from the minor premise being impossible: there is no atom that has not been preceded by non-existence. The author then argues that if something is pre-eternal, it cannot cease to exist because that would require a particularizer, making it originated. Since the minor premise is impossible, its contradictory (every atom is preceded by non-existence) is true, and that conclusion is equivalent to 'the world is originated.' Thus the syllogism illustrates producing an equivalent claim, though the minor premise is false in reality.

A syllogism with an impossible premise yields a false conclusion, but the contradictory of that impossible premise can be logically equivalent to the desired claim.

Detecting Impossible Premises in Categorical Syllogisms
Author analyzes a flawed syllogism about atoms and pre-eternity, showing the minor premise is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a categorical syllogism contains an impossible premise and explain why the argument fails, using the example from the passage.

Core Idea
An impossible premise makes any conclusion from it irrelevant, but its contradictory is necessarily true.

In this passage, the author examines a syllogism about atoms and pre-eternity. The conclusion is false, but the real problem is that one premise cannot possibly be true. Understanding this logical flaw helps you evaluate arguments in Islamic theological debates, where impossible premises can slip into proofs if not carefully checked.

Guided Path
1
Identify the argument's components
First, find the conclusion and the premises. The passage states the conclusion: 'All that is supposed not to have been preceded by non-existence is perishable.' The minor premise is: 'Every atom that is supposed not to have been preceded by non-existence is pre-eternal.' The major premise is implicit: pre-eternal beings are imperishable.
The major premise is based on the author's statement that the pre-eternal cannot perish.
2
Check each premise for possibility
Is the minor premise possible? The author argues that no atom exists without being preceded by non-existence, because atoms are observed to perish gradually. Therefore, the premise 'Every atom not preceded by non-existence is pre-eternal' is impossible—it cannot be true under any circumstance.
A premise is impossible if it contradicts established facts or necessary truths, not merely if it is false.
3
Determine the effect on the syllogism
Since the minor premise is impossible, the syllogism is unsound, even if it is formally valid. A valid syllogism with an impossible premise does not give a reliable conclusion. The conclusion may be false, as here.
Soundness requires both validity and true premises.
4
Derive the necessary truth from impossibility
The impossibility of the minor premise entails that its contradictory must be true. The contradictory is: 'Every atom is preceded by non-existence.' This directly leads to the claim that the world is originated (hadith), which is the desired conclusion in kalām.
This logical move is an immediate inference: from an impossible proposition, its negation is necessarily true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جميع المفروض أنها لم تسبق بعدم بمنعدمة، وهذه النتيجة كاذبة ; لأن ذرات العالم تنعدم شيئًا فشيئًا كما هو مشاهد، وإنما كذبت النتيجة لكون الصغرى مستحيلة، وهي قوله : (كل ذرة من العالم فُرض أنها لم تُسبق بعدم فهي أزلية) ; لاستحالة وجود ذرة منه لم تُسبق بعدم حتى تكون أزلية، ومن المعلوم أن الأزلي الوجودي لا يُعقل انعدامه ; إذ لو كان انعدامه جائزا لكان محتاجًا في وجوده الأول إلى مخصص يخصص وجوده، ويرجحه على عدمه المساويه عقلاً أزلاً، فيكون حادثا لترجيح المخصص المذكور وجودَه على عدمه، والفرض أنه أزلي، واستحالة الصغرى التي هي كل ذرة من العالم فُرض أنها لم تُسبق بعدم فهي أزلية يلزمها صحة نقيضها، وهي (كل ذرة من ذرات العالم مسبوقة بعدم) ; لتساوي ذراته لذاتها، فهذه النتيجة مثال لإنتاج الدليل مساوي الدعوى الممنوعة ; لأنها مساوية لقولك : العالم حادث
All that is supposed not to have been preceded by non-existence is perishable, and this conclusion is false because the atoms of the world perish little by little as is observed. But the conclusion is false only because the minor premise is impossible, namely his statement: 'Every atom of the world that is supposed not to have been preceded by non-existence is pre-eternal' because it is impossible for there to exist an atom that was not preceded by non-existence so that it would be pre-eternal. And it is known that the existent that is pre-eternal cannot be conceived to perish; for if its perishing were possible, it would have been in need in its first existence of a determiner to determine its existence and preponderate it over its non-existence which is equally possible mentally from pre-eternity, so it would be originated due to the preponderance of that determiner of its existence over its non-existence, while the assumption is that it is pre-eternal. The impossibility of the minor premise, which is 'Every atom of the world that is supposed not to have been preceded by non-existence is pre-eternal', entails the truth of its contradictory, which is 'Every atom of the atoms of the world is preceded by non-existence' because the atoms are equal in themselves. This conclusion is an example of producing a proof equal to the prohibited claim, because it is equal to your statement: 'The world is originated.'
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of a faulty syllogism in a dialectical context. The author describes an argument that attempts to prove something about atoms and pre-eternity. The conclusion drawn is that 'everything supposed not to have been preceded by non-existence is perishable,' but the author immediately identifies this conclusion as false, citing observable evidence that atoms perish gradually. The reason for the false conclusion, however, is not an error in logical form but the impossibility of the minor premise. The minor premise asserts that 'every atom supposed not to have been preceded by non-existence is pre-eternal.' But no such atom can exist, because every atom is preceded by non-existence. The author then explains that if something were truly pre-eternal, its perishing would be inconceivable because its existence would not have required a determiner to bring it into being from equal alternatives; but if perishing were possible, it would imply it was originated, contradicting the assumption of pre-eternity. Thus, the minor premise is impossible, and from impossibility, the contradictory necessarily follows: 'Every atom is preceded by non-existence.' This contradictory conclusion is precisely the statement 'The world is originated,' which is the very claim the opponent likely wanted to prove. The author uses this example to illustrate a fallacy in disputation: producing a proof that yields the same conclusion as the contested claim, but which is itself flawed due to an impossible premise.

Debate on Ru'yat Allah: From Possibility to Actual Occurrence via First-Figure Syllogism
Author applies first-figure syllogism to prove actual occurrence of ru'yat Allah, implying possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyse a dialectical exchange on the possibility of seeing Allah and identify the first-figure categorical syllogism used to prove actual occurrence, then deduce its implication for possibility.

Core Idea
Proving actual occurrence automatically proves possibility — a logical shortcut in theological debate.

This passage applies the principles of categorical syllogism and jadal (dialectical debate) to one of the most famous theological disputes in Islamic thought: whether Allah will be seen on the Day of Resurrection. The author clarifies that the debate is over possibility, not actuality, and shows how Ahl al-Sunna can prove possibility by proving actual occurrence through a Qur'anic argument in the first figure. This lesson will walk you through the structure of the debate and the logical form of the proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed claim (mudda'a)
The Mu'tazila deny the possibility (imkān) of seeing Allah; Ahl al-Sunna affirm it. Note that the debate is about possibility, not actual occurrence.
This sets the stage for the logical move: proving actuality suffices.
2
Extract the mu'allil's strategy
The mu'allil (prover) aims to prove actual occurrence (iṭlāq), which is more specific (akhaṣṣ) than possibility. Since the existence of the specific entails the existence of the general, proving actuality establishes possibility.
3
Analyse the categorical syllogism
The syllogism: Major premise - Everything Allah establishes in the Qur'an is true; Minor premise - Allah established the actual occurrence of vision (Qur'an 75:22-23); Conclusion - The vision is true (actual). This is first figure (Barbara).
The middle term is 'what Allah has established in His book'.
4
Evaluate the dialectical move
By proving actuality, the mu'allil forces the opponent to either accept actuality (which implies possibility) or deny the Qur'anic text — an untenable position for the Mu'tazila who accept the Qur'an.
The author notes that counter-arguments exist but are not addressed here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا علمت ذلك فاعلم أن المناظرة بين أهل السنة والجماعة القائلين برؤية الله جل وعلا بالأبصار يوم القيامة وبين المعتزلة المانعين لذلك إنما هي في جهة الإمكان، فالمعتزلة يقولون إنها مستحيلة ولا تدخل في الإمكان أصلاً، وأهل السنة يقولون هي ممكنة؛ وطلب موسى لها في قوله رَبِّ أَرِنِي أَنظُرْ إِلَيْكَ دليل على إمكانها؛ لأن نبي الله موسى عليه وعلى نبينا الصلاة والسلام لا يجهل المستحيل في حق الله تعالى، ولا يلتبس عليه بالممكن حتى يسأله. وإذا علمت أن المناظرة في الإمكان علمت أن المعلل إذا أقام دليلاً ينتج الإطلاق، الذي هو الوقوع الفعلي، الذي هو أخص قطعا من الإمكان، فكأنه أقام الدليل على الإمكان؛ لأن وجود الأخص يستلزم وجود الأعم. وإذا علمت ذلك فاعلم أن صورة استدلاله على ذلك أن يقول: الله أثبت في كتابه وقوع الرؤية بالفعل في قوله: {وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَذٍ نَاضِرَةٌ إِلَى رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ} [القيامة/22-23] فصح بنظرها إلى ربها بالفعل يوم القيامة، وكل ما أثبته الله في كتابه فهو حق صحيح، ينتج من الشكل الأول: الرؤية حق صحيح، وهذه النتيجة أخص من الدعوى الممنوعة، التي هي الإمكان، فافهمه؛ لأنه لا يقع بالفعل إلا ما هو ممكن الوقوع، ومن المعلوم بالضرورة أن المستحيل وقوعه عقلاً لا يقع بالفعل، كما لا يخفى. ومناقشة استدلال المعتزلة ببعض الآيات كقوله تعالى: {لا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَارُ} [الأنعام/103] وقوله: {لَن تَرَانِي} [الأعراف/143]، ونحو ذلك قد بينا بطلانها في غير هذا الموضع، ومقصودنا هنا مطلق المثال.
When you have known that, know that the debate between Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jamāʿa, who affirm seeing Allah the Exalted with the eyes on the Day of Resurrection, and the Muʿtazila who deny it, is in the aspect of possibility. For the Muʿtazila say it is impossible and does not enter into possibility at all, while Ahl al-Sunna say it is possible. And Moses' request in his saying, 'My Lord, show me [Yourself] that I may look at You' [Surah al-Aʿrāf 7:143] is evidence of its possibility, because the Prophet of Allah Moses (peace be upon him) does not ignore what is impossible with respect to Allah, nor does he confuse it with the possible so as to ask for it. And when you know that the debate is about possibility, you know that if the one providing proof (al-muʿallil) produces a proof that yields the absolute (al-iṭlāq), which is actual occurrence, which is certainly more specific than possibility, then it is as if he has produced proof for possibility, because the existence of the more specific entails the existence of the more general. And when you know that, know that the form of his argument for that is that he says: Allah has established in His book the actual occurrence of vision in His saying: 'Faces on that day will be radiant, looking at their Lord' [Surah al-Qiyāma 75:22-23]. So it is established that they look at Him actually on the Day of Resurrection. And everything that Allah has established in His book is true and correct. So by the first figure [the conclusion] follows: the vision is true and correct. And this result is more specific than the contested claim, which is possibility. So understand this, because what actually occurs is only what is possible to occur, and it is necessarily known that what is rationally impossible does not occur actually, as is not hidden. And the discussion of the Muʿtazila's argumentation with some verses like His saying, 'Sight does not comprehend Him' [Surah al-Anʿām 6:103] and His saying, 'You will not see Me' [Surah al-Aʿrāf 7:143], and similar, we have demonstrated their invalidity elsewhere, and our purpose here is simply the example.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage applies the previous logical principle to a real theological dispute: whether Allah will be seen on the Day of Resurrection. The author clarifies that the disagreement between Ahl al-Sunna and Mu'tazila is not about actual occurrence but about possibility (al-imkān). The Mu'tazila claim it is rationally impossible (mustaḥīl), while Ahl al-Sunna affirm its possibility. To support possibility, they cite Prophet Musa's request to see Allah, arguing that a prophet would not ask for what he knows to be impossible. Then the author explains a key tactic: if the muʿallil (the one providing proof) can produce evidence for the actual occurrence (al-iṭlāq) of the vision, that automatically proves its possibility, because the actual entails the possible. He then presents a syllogism in the first figure: the Qur'an explicitly states that faces will look at their Lord on that day (actual occurrence); everything the Qur'an establishes is true; therefore, the vision is true. This conclusion is more specific than the claim of possibility, but it suffices to rebut the Mu'tazila's denial of possibility. The author notes that he will not engage in refuting the Mu'tazila's counter-arguments in detail here, as this is merely an example.

Key Terms
المعلل
الإطلاق
الإمكان
الشكل الأول

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the actual is more general than the possible.
B) Because the possible is more specific than the actual.
C) Because the actual is more specific than the possible, so actuality entails possibility.
D) Because possibility entails actuality.

Key Takeaway
In dialectical theology, proving the actual occurrence of a claimed event directly proves its possibility, because the specific always implies the general, and this can be structured as a valid first-figure categorical syllogism.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Mu'aradah bi al-Mithl in Dialectical Syllogisms
Author illustrates counter-argument by similarity with first-figure syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a mu'aradah bi al-mithl counter-argument using a first-figure categorical syllogism on a theological issue.

Core Idea
Mu'aradah bi al-mithl forces a parallel syllogism to expose contradictory implications without denying premises.

In dialectical debates, a common tactic is to meet the opponent's argument with a structurally identical syllogism that leads to the opposite conclusion. This passage demonstrates that technique using the classical debate on the world's eternity. The prover argues the world is eternal; the objector counters with a parallel syllogism concluding the world is originated. Both syllogisms are first-figure categorical, yet they yield contradictory results. Understanding this technique equips you to defend or challenge theological positions effectively.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prover's syllogism
Extract the two premises and the conclusion from the prover's argument: 'The world is an effect of the Eternal' (minor premise), 'Everything that is an effect of the Eternal is eternal' (major premise), therefore 'The world is eternal' (conclusion).
2
Determine the figure and mood
Check that this is a first-figure categorical syllogism: the minor premise has the subject 'world' and predicate 'effect of the Eternal'; the major premise has subject 'effect of the Eternal' and predicate 'eternal'. The middle term 'effect of the Eternal' is the subject of the major and predicate of the minor. This is first figure, and the mood is AAA (Barbara) since both premises and conclusion are universal affirmatives.
The passage states it is first-figure iqtirani.
3
Identify the middle term and its role
The middle term is 'effect of the Eternal'. It links the two premises but does not appear in the conclusion. In the objector's counter-argument, a different middle term must be used to produce a contradictory conclusion while preserving the first-figure form.
4
Construct the objector's parallel syllogism
The objector replaces the middle term with 'changing' and adjusts the premises: 'The world is changing' (minor), 'Everything changing is originated' (major), therefore 'The world is originated' (conclusion). This is also first-figure AAA. The conclusion directly contradicts the prover's conclusion.
The objector does not deny the prover's premises; he offers an alternative syllogism of the same form.
5
Compare the two syllogisms
Notice that both syllogisms have the same subject (the world) and the same logical form (first-figure AAA), but different middle terms and predicates in the conclusion. This forces the prover to defend his universal premise (every effect of the Eternal is eternal) or the minor (the world is an effect of the Eternal).
The debate is now at a standstill until the prover responds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
استُثني فيه نقيض التالي فأنتج نقيض المقدم كما ترى . ومن أمثلة المعارضة بالمثل ما قدمناه من قول معتقد قدم العالم : العالم أثر القديم، وكل ما هو أثر القديم فهو قديم، ينتج في زعمه : العالم قديم . فيعارضه السائل بالمثل فيقول : العالم متغير، وكل متغير حادث، ينتج : العالم حادث . فكل واحد من دليل المعلل ودليل المعارض اقتراني من الشكل الأول، وإن اختلف فيهما الحد الوسط والمقدمتان كما أوضحناه .
It is excluded [by] the contradictory of the consequent, so the contradictory of the antecedent results, as you see. And from examples of counter-argument by similarity is what we previously mentioned from the statement of the believer in the eternity of the world: 'The world is an effect of the Eternal, and everything that is an effect of the Eternal is eternal.' According to his claim, it produces: 'The world is eternal.' So the objector counters him by similarity, saying: 'The world is changing, and everything changing is originated.' It produces: 'The world is originated.' So each one of the proof of the prover and the proof of the objector is a conjunctive syllogism of the first figure, although the middle term and the two premises differ in them, as we explained.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage begins with a reminder of the rule in conditional syllogism: when the consequent is negated (istithna' al-naqid), the antecedent is also negated. Then it offers a concrete example of mu'aradah bi al-mithl from the theological debate on the world's eternity. The prover (mu'allil) argues: 'The world is an effect of the Eternal; every effect of the Eternal is eternal; therefore the world is eternal.' The objector (sa'il) counters with a syllogism of the same form: 'The world is changing; everything changing is originated (hadith); therefore the world is originated.' Both syllogisms are iqtirani (categorical) in the first figure, but they have different middle terms (the middle in the first is 'effect of the Eternal', in the second is 'changing') and different premises. The author emphasizes that this is a valid dialectical move—the objector does not deny the prover's premises but produces a parallel argument leading to an opposite conclusion.

Key Terms
معارضة بالمثل
اقتراني
الشكل الأول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The effect of the Eternal
B) The world
C) Changing
D) Originated

Key Takeaway
Mu'aradah bi al-mithl is a dialectical tool that mirrors the prover's syllogistic form to produce a contradictory conclusion, forcing the prover to defend or revise his premises.

The First Syllogistic Figure in Ibrahim's Quranic Argument
Al-Bannani citing Ibrahim's argument as first figure example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the first-figure categorical syllogism potentially present in Prophet Ibrahim's argument with Nimrod as explained by al-Bannani.

Core Idea
Ibrahim's Quranic debate contains a first-figure syllogism potentially — logic embedded in revelation.

Al-Bannani, commenting on al-Akhdari's Sullam, asserts that the three figures of the categorical syllogism exist potentially (bil-quwwa) in the Quran. He takes Prophet Ibrahim's argument against Nimrod's claim to lordship as an explicit instance of the first figure. By analyzing this passage, you will learn how to extract a formal categorical syllogism from a dialectical Quranic exchange, revealing the logical rigor of prophetic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Quranic context
Locate the verse (Quran 2:258) and the exchange: Ibrahim says 'My Lord gives life and causes death.' Nimrod counters by killing and sparing a man. Ibrahim then moves to the sun argument.
The passage focuses on the first figure, so the initial life/death statement is key.
2
Extract the implicit universal premise
From Ibrahim's statement 'My Lord is He who gives life and causes death', derive the universal proposition: 'Every being that gives life and causes death is the (true) Lord.'
This is the major premise of the first-figure syllogism.
3
Identify the instantial premise
Ibrahim's statement also implies: 'Allah (my Lord) gives life and causes death.' This is the minor premise, identifying the individual that falls under the universal.
The middle term is 'gives life and causes death'.
4
Form the first-figure syllogism
Combine premises: Major: Every being that gives life and death is Lord. Minor: Allah gives life and death. Conclusion: Therefore, Allah is the Lord. This is Barbara (AAA) in the first figure.
The first figure requires the middle term to be subject in major and predicate in minor.
5
Reflect on the dialectical move
Nimrod's mimicry does not refute the syllogism; Ibrahim then shifts to a property only Allah possesses (bringing the sun) to demonstrate the conclusion more powerfully.
The initial syllogism remains valid; the shift is a jadal strategy (intiqal).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في آيات قرآنية تستلزم طرق المناظرة المصطلح عليها
اعلم أولاً أن العلامة الشيخ محمد بن الحسن البناني قال في شرحه لسلم الأخضري : ذكروا أن الأشكال الثلاثة موجودة ـ أي بالقوة - في القرآن العظيم :
أما الأول : ففي احتجاج إبراهيم الخليل ـ عليه وعلى نبينا الصلاة والسلام - على انفراد الله بالربوبية ونفيها عن النمرود، حيث ادعاها وقال للخليل : من ربك؟ فقال: ربي الذي يحي ويميت. فأحضر رجلين فقتل أحدهما وترك الآخر، وقال: أنا أحي وأميت؛ فهذا أمته وهذا أحيته . فانتقل [به] الخليل إلى ما [؟] يتعلق به كسب المخلوق فقال : إن الله يأتي بالشمس من المشرق فأت بها من المغرب، فبهت الذي كفر، وانحسمت شبهته .
Chapter on Quranic verses that necessitate the methods of disputation as conventionally accepted. Know first that the scholar Shaykh Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Bannani said in his commentary on al-Akhdari's Sullam: They mentioned that the three figures exist—i.e., potentially—in the Great Qur'an. As for the first: It is in the argument of Ibrahim al-Khalil—peace and blessings upon him and our Prophet—for the exclusivity of Allah in lordship and its negation from Nimrod, when [Nimrod] claimed it and said to the Khalil: 'Who is your Lord?' He said: 'My Lord is He who gives life and causes death.' So [Nimrod] brought two men, killed one and left the other, and said: 'I give life and cause death; this one I killed and this one I gave life.' Then the Khalil moved to what is not within the scope of the creature's acquisition and said: 'Indeed Allah brings the sun from the east, so bring it from the west.' So the disbeliever was confounded, and his doubt was dispelled.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage is a citation from al-Bannani's commentary on the Sullam al-Murawnaq, asserting that the three standard figures of the categorical syllogism (al-ashkal al-thalatha) are found potentially (bil-quwwa) in the Qur'an. It then provides the example of Prophet Ibrahim's debate with Nimrod as an instance of the first figure. The argument proceeds: Ibrahim first states that his Lord gives life and causes death (a premise). Nimrod attempts to refute this by killing one man and sparing another, claiming that he too can give life and death. Ibrahim then shifts the ground to an act no human can perform—bringing the sun from the west—thereby exposing Nimrod's inability and demonstrating that true lordship belongs only to Allah. This is a classic dialectical move (intiqal) from one premise to another, which the logicians interpret as a categorical syllogism: the major premise (only Allah can control the sun), the minor premise (Nimrod cannot), leading to the conclusion (Nimrod is not Lord). The passage underscores the compatibility of Aristotelian logic with Quranic reasoning, a common theme in post-Ghazalian Islamic thought.

بالقوة
احتجاج

Ibrahim's Quranic argument contains a first-figure categorical syllogism potentially, demonstrating that prophetic reasoning is logically rigorous.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Extracting Categorical Syllogisms from the Quran
Al-Bannani analyzes a Quranic syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to extract a categorical syllogism from a Quranic statement and verify its validity using contraposition.

Core Idea
Every Quranic argument can be formalized into a valid syllogism—even Ibrahim's debate with Nimrod follows first-figure logic.

In this lesson, we explore how the classical logician al-Bannani extracted a full categorical syllogism from a single Quranic verse. Prophet Ibrahim's argument against Nimrod is not merely rhetorical; it contains a rigorous logical structure that can be analyzed using the tools of qiyas. Understanding this deepens our appreciation of the Qur'an's precision and provides a model for formalizing prophetic arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
Nimrod claimed lordship (rububiyya). Ibrahim first refutes him by an argument about life and death, then moves to a second argument about the sun. Focus on the second argument: Ibrahim challenges Nimrod to bring the sun from the west.
The claim being refuted is 'Nimrod is a lord'.
2
Locate the explicit command
Ibrahim says: 'Indeed Allah brings the sun from the east, so bring it from the west.' This is a command of incapacitation (amr ta'jiz). It implies that Nimrod cannot bring the sun from the west.
The command functions as the minor premise in the syllogism.
3
Derive the minor premise
From the command, we infer 'You (Nimrod) are not able to bring the sun from the west.' This is the minor premise (sughra). It is an affirmative modified proposition (mujiba ma'dula) with a negated predicate.
The middle term will be 'not able'.
4
Derive the major premise via contraposition
The major premise is derived from the implied premise 'My Lord (Allah) is able to bring the sun from the west.' Through contraposition (aks al-naqid), we obtain: 'Everyone who is not able to bring the sun from the west is not a lord.' This is the major premise (kubra).
Nimrod accepts both premises, so the conclusion follows necessarily.
5
Construct the full syllogism and check validity
The syllogism is: Major (kubra): All who are not able to bring the sun from the west are not lords. Minor (sughra): You (Nimrod) are not able to bring the sun from the west. Conclusion: Therefore, you are not a lord. This is a valid first-figure syllogism (mood AII, with minor as affirmative modified). The middle term is 'able to bring from the west' (in its negated form).
The author also suggests a more elegant formulation using 'incapable' to make the major a universal negative, which would be mood EIO.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في آيات قرآنية تستلزم طرق المناظرة المصطلح عليها اعلم أولاً أن العلامة الشيخ محمد بن الحسن البناني قال في شرحه لسلم الأخضري : ذكروا أن الأشكال الثلاثة موجودة ـ أي بالقوة - في القرآن العظيم : أما الأول : ففي احتجاج إبراهيم الخليل ـ عليه وعلى نبينا الصلاة والسلام - على انفراد الله بالربوبية ونفيها عن النمرود، حيث ادعاها وقال للخليل : من ربك؟ فقال: ربي الذي يحي ويميت. فأحضر رجلين فقتل أحدهما وترك الآخر، وقال: أنا أحي وأميت؛ فهذا أمته وهذا أحيته . فانتقل الخليل إلى ما يتعلق به كسب المخلوق فقال : إن الله يأتي بالشمس من المشرق فأت بها من المغرب، فبهت الذي كفر، وانحسمت شبهته . فقوله فإنَّ اللهَ يَأْتِي بِالشَّمْسِ من المشرق فَأْتِ بِهَا مِنَ الْمَغْرِبِ [البقرة/ ٢٥٨]: في قوة قوله : أنت لا تقدر أن تأتي بالشمس من المغرب، وكل من لا يقدر على ذلك فليس برب، ينتج : أنت لست برب . فالصغرى يمكن أن تؤخذ من قوله فأت بها من المغرب؛ لأنه أمر تعجيز، وتؤخذ أيضًا من شاهد حال النمرود ، ولا يسعه إنكارها . والكبرى عكس نقيض قضية مفهومه من قوله: ﴿فَإِنَّ اللَّهَ يَأْتِي بِالشَّمْسِ مِنَ الْمَشْرِقِ فَأْتِ بِهَا مِنَ الْمَغْرِبِ﴾ : وهي : ربي يقدر على أن يأتي بالشمس من المغرب أي كما أتى بها من المشرق. فيُعكس بعكس النقيض الموافق إلى الكبرى، والنمرود أيضًا يسلمها، فلهذا بهت . والظاهر أن قولهم في الصغرى: أنت لا تقدر) موجبة معدولة؛ ليكون الوسط مكرراً ، وقولهم في الكبرى : (وكل من لا يقدر) محمولة سالبة، وإن لم تسوّر بالسور المتعارف للكلية السالبة؛ لأن المعتبر المعنى لا اللفظ ولو جعل (عاجز) مكان (لا يقدر ) ويقال في الكبرى: (ولا شيء من العاجز برب لكان أولى . ثم يصح سوق الدليل استثنائيًا فيقال: لو كنت يا نمرود ربي
Chapter on Quranic verses that entail the agreed-upon methods of disputation. Know first that the scholar Sheikh Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Bannani said in his commentary on al-Sullam al-Akhdari: They mentioned that the three figures exist—potentially—in the Great Quran. As for the first: it is in the argument of Ibrahim al-Khalil—peace be upon him and upon our Prophet—regarding God's exclusive lordship and denying it to Nimrod, when he claimed it and said to the Khalil: Who is your Lord? He said: My Lord is the one who gives life and causes death. So he brought two men, killed one and left the other, and said: I give life and cause death; this one I killed and this one I gave life. Then the Khalil turned to what involves the creature's acquisition and said: Indeed Allah brings the sun from the east, so bring it from the west. Then the disbeliever was confounded, and his doubt was eliminated. For His statement: 'Indeed Allah brings the sun from the east, so bring it from the west' [al-Baqara/258] is equivalent to His statement: You are not able to bring the sun from the west, and everyone who is not able to do that is not a lord, resulting: You are not a lord. The minor premise can be taken from His statement 'so bring it from the west' because it is a command of incapacitation, and it is also taken from the witnessed state of Nimrod, and he cannot deny it. The major premise is the converse by contradiction of the proposition understood from His statement: 'Indeed Allah brings the sun from the east, so bring it from the west', which is: My Lord is able to bring the sun from the west, just as He brought it from the east. So it is converted by the corresponding contrapositive to the major premise, and Nimrod also concedes it, so he was confounded. It appears that their statement in the minor: 'You are not able' is an affirmative modified proposition so that the middle term is repeated, and their statement in the major: 'And everyone who is not able' is a negative simple proposition, even if it is not quantified with the usual quantifier for universal negative; because what matters is the meaning, not the wording. And if one made 'incapable' in place of 'not able' and said in the major: 'And no incapable is a lord', it would be more appropriate. Then it is valid to present the proof as a conditional; one would say: O Nimrod, if you were a lord, then you would be able to bring the sun from the west.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage is a syllogistic analysis of the Quranic argument of Prophet Ibrahim against Nimrod. It opens with a chapter on Quranic verses that contain the methods of disputation (al-munazara). The author cites al-Bannani's commentary, who states that the three figures of the categorical syllogism exist potentially in the Quran. He gives the first figure example: Ibrahim's argument. The story is summarized: Nimrod claimed lordship, Ibrahim said his Lord gives life and death, Nimrod countered by killing and sparing, so Ibrahim shifted to a different argument—God brings the sun from the east, so Nimrod should bring it from the west. The author extracts a syllogism from this: 'You cannot bring the sun from the west; everyone who cannot do so is not a lord; therefore you are not a lord.' The minor premise is derived from the command 'Bring it from the west' (a command of incapacitation), and from the observed situation of Nimrod. The major premise is obtained by contraposition (عكس النقيض) from the implied premise that Ibrahim's Lord is able to bring the sun from the west. The author discusses the logical form: the minor 'You are not able' is an affirmative modified proposition (موجبة معدولة) to have a repeated middle term, and the major 'Everyone who is not able' is a universal negative (سالبة كلية) even if not explicitly quantified. He suggests a better formulation using 'incapable' to make it a standard universal negative. He also shows that the argument can be expressed as a conditional syllogism: 'If you were a lord, you would be able to bring the sun from the west; but you are not able; so you are not a lord.' This analysis demonstrates how Quranic arguments can be formalized in Aristotelian logic.

Key Terms
صغرى (sughra)
كبرى (kubra)
عكس النقيض (aks al-naqid)
موجبة معدولة (mujiba ma'dula)
استثنائي (istithna'i)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the syllogism extracted from Ibrahim's argument, what is the minor premise?
A) 'Everyone who is not able to bring the sun from the west is not a lord.'
B) 'You are not able to bring the sun from the west.'
C) 'God brings the sun from the east.'
D) 'Nimrod is a lord.'

Key Takeaway
Prophetic arguments in the Quran can be formalized into valid categorical syllogisms, demonstrating the harmony of revelation and reason.

The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
First-Figure Categorical Syllogism from Ibrahim's Argument
Author's analysis of the first-figure form

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to extract the first-figure categorical syllogism implicit in Ibrahim's argument against Nimrod and verify its validity.

Core Idea
The first figure yields the conclusion directly from incapacitation and lordship.

This passage introduces the first-figure categorical syllogism underlying the Quranic verse. We will see how the minor premise is derived from the incapacitating command, and the major premise via contrapositive, leading to a valid refutation of Nimrod's claim to divinity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion
What is the final claim that Ibrahim proves against Nimrod? The conclusion is 'You are not a lord' (أنت لست برب). This is the target of the syllogism.
The conclusion is stated explicitly at the end of the first sentence.
2
Extract the minor premise
The minor premise comes from the incapacitating command 'bring it from the west.' Since Nimrod cannot do so, it establishes: 'You are not able to bring the sun from the west.' This is a negative minor premise.
The author notes this can be taken directly from the command or from Nimrod's observed inability.
3
Derive the major premise via contrapositive
The major premise is obtained by contrapositive (عكس النقيض) of Ibrahim's statement that his Lord can bring the sun from the west. That gives: 'Everyone who is not able to bring the sun from the west is not a lord.'
This operation converts an affirmative capacity claim into a universal negative rule.
4
Verify middle term repetition
The middle term is 'not able' (لا يقدر). In the minor, it is an affirmative with a negated predicate (mawjuba ma'dula), and in the major, it is a simple negative (muhassala saliba). This ensures the middle term is repeated, satisfying the first-figure condition.
The author suggests using 'incapable' (عاجز) to make the middle term clearer.
5
Check validity of the first figure
First-figure categorical syllogisms are valid when the major premise is universal and the minor is affirmative. Here the major is universal negative, the minor is affirmative (though with a negated predicate), and the conclusion is universal negative. This is a valid mood (Celarent-like).
The form is: No M is P; All S is M; Therefore no S is P.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقوله فإنَّ اللهَ يَأْتِي بِالشَّمْسِس ) الآية [البقرة/ ٢٥٨]: في قوة قوله : أنت لا تقدر أن تأتي بالشمس من المغرب، وكل من لا يقدر على ذلك فليس برب، ينتج : أنت لست برب . فالصغرى يمكن أن تؤخذ من قوله فأت بها من المغرب)؛ لأنه أمر تعجيز، وتؤخذ أيضًا من شاهد حال النمرود ـ لعنه الله ، ولا يسعه إنكارها . والكبرى عكس نقيض قضية مفهومه من قوله: ﴿فَإِنَّ اللَّهَ يَأْتِي بِالشَّمْسِ مِنَ الْمَشْرِقِ فَأْتِ بِهَا مِنَ الْمَغْرِبِ ) : وهي : ربي يقدر على أن يأتي بالشمس من المغرب أي كما أتى بها من المشرق. فيُعكس [ بعكس ] (١) النقيض الموافق إلى الكبرى، والنمرود أيضًا يسلمها، فلهذا بهت . والظاهر أن قولهم في الصغرى: أنت لا تقدر) موجبة معدولة؛ ليكون الوسط مكرراً ، وقولهم في الكبرى : (وكل من لا يقدر) محصلة سالبة، وإن لم تسوّر بالسور المتعارف للكلية السالبة؛ لأن المعتبر المعنى لا اللفظ ولو جعل (عاجز) مكان (لا يقدر ) ويقال في الكبرى: (ولا شيء من العاجز برب لكان أولى . ثم يصح سوق الدليل استثنائيًا فيقال: لو كنت يا نمرود ربي لقدرت على الإتيان بالشمس من المغرب لكنك لا تقدر عليه، ينتج : فلست بربي . ويمكن أن يساق من الشكل الثاني بأن يقال : ما أنت قادر على أن تأتي بالشمس من المغرب وربي قادر على أن يأتي بها، ينتج : فلست بربي؛ فليس المقصود حصر المستنبط من الآية في الشكل الأول، وكأنهم رأوه أظهر من غيره .
His saying: 'For indeed Allah brings the sun...' the verse [Al-Baqarah 2:258] is equivalent to his saying: 'You are not able to bring the sun from the west, and everyone who is not able to do that is not a lord,' so it yields: 'You are not a lord.' The minor premise can be taken from his saying: 'Then bring it from the west,' because it is an incapacitating command, and it is also taken from the observable state of Nimrod — may Allah curse him — and he cannot deny it. And the major premise is the contrapositive of the proposition understood from his saying: 'Indeed Allah brings the sun from the east, so bring it from the west' — that is: 'My lord is able to bring the sun from the west' i.e., as He brought it from the east. So it is converted by the contrapositive into the major, and Nimrod also concedes it, therefore he was confounded. And apparently their statement in the minor: 'You are not able' is an affirmative quantified negative (mawjuba ma'dula) so that the middle term is repeated, and their statement in the major: 'And everyone who is not able' is a simple negative (muhassala saliba), even though it is not quantified with the conventional quantifier for universal negative; because what matters is the meaning, not the wording. And if one made 'incapable' instead of 'not able' and said in the major: 'And no incapable person is a lord,' it would be better. Then it is valid to conduct the proof as exceptive (istithna'i) saying: 'If you, O Nimrod, were my lord, you would be able to bring the sun from the west, but you are not able to do so,' so it yields: 'You are not my lord.' And it can be conducted from the second figure by saying: 'You are not able to bring the sun from the west, and my lord is able to bring it,' so it yields: 'You are not my lord.' So the intention is not to restrict what is deduced from the verse to the first figure, and it seems they considered it more apparent than others.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage is a detailed expansion showing how the Quranic argument of Ibrahim against Nimrod (Surah Al-Baqarah 2:258) can be expressed in multiple syllogistic forms, specifically the first figure, the exceptive syllogism (qiyas istithna'i), and the second figure. The author begins by explaining the implicit syllogism in the verse: 'You are not able to bring the sun from the west, and everyone unable is not a lord, therefore you are not a lord.' This is a first-figure categorical syllogism. He then derives the minor premise from Nimrod's inability (taken from the incapacitating command 'bring it from the west') and the major premise from Ibrahim's implied claim that his Lord can bring the sun from the west, converted to a universal negative via contrapositive. The author notes that the minor is technically an affirmative quantified negative (mawjuba ma'dula) and the major is a simple negative, and suggests replacing 'not able' with 'incapable' for clarity. Next, he shows the exceptive form: 'If you were my lord, you would be able; but you are not able; therefore you are not my lord.' This is a conditional syllogism (antecendent-consequent). Finally, he presents a second-figure formulation: 'You are not able, and my lord is able, therefore you are not my lord.' This is a second-figure categorical syllogism. The author concludes that the deduction from the verse is not limited to the first figure, though that figure may be the most apparent.

The first-figure categorical syllogism from Ibrahim's argument uses the incapacitating command to establish the minor premise and contrapositive for the major, yielding a valid refutation.

The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
Exceptive Syllogism from Ibrahim's Argument
Author's presentation of the exceptive form

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the exceptive (istithna'i) syllogism in Ibrahim's argument and verify that it is a valid modus tollens.

Core Idea
The exceptive form uses a conditional and the denial of the consequent to negate the antecedent.

After the first-figure form, the author presents the same argument as an exceptive syllogism: 'If you were my lord, you would be able; but you are not able; therefore you are not my lord.' This is a classic modus tollens, and we will see how it is constructed and why it is valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premise
The conditional premise is: 'If you were my lord, you would be able to bring the sun from the west.' This is an 'if-then' statement linking lordship to ability.
The author uses the phrase 'لو كنت يا نمرود ربي' to introduce the antecedent.
2
Identify the exception (al-istithna')
The exception is the denial of the consequent: 'You are not able to do so.' This is the second part of the exceptive syllogism.
The exception is stated as 'لكنك لا تقدر عليه'.
3
Determine the logical form
This is modus tollens: If P then Q; not Q; therefore not P. Here P = 'You are my lord', Q = 'You are able'.
Modus tollens is a valid form of conditional reasoning.
4
Verify the conclusion follows
From the conditional and the denial of the consequent, the denial of the antecedent necessarily follows: 'Therefore you are not my lord.' This is logically sound.
This form is valid regardless of the truth of the premises; it preserves truth.

Better: Use modus ponens. But for modus tollens, use: 'If the dog did not drink, then no washing; but washing occurred; therefore the dog did drink.' That is affirming the consequent? Hmm. The Syllogism

Second-Figure Categorical Syllogism from Ibrahim's Argument
Author's presentation of the second-figure form

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the second-figure categorical syllogism from Ibrahim's argument and identify its unique condition (one negative premise).

Core Idea
The second figure proves that Nimrod is not a lord by using the middle term as predicate in both premises.

Finally, the author shows the argument in the second figure: 'You are not able to bring the sun from the west, and my lord is able to bring it, therefore you are not my lord.' Here, the middle term is 'able to bring the sun', and we will check why this form is valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
The premises are: (1) 'You are not able to bring the sun from the west' (negative premise). (2) 'My lord is able to bring the sun from the west' (affirmative premise).
Note that the subject of the first premise is 'you' and the second is 'my lord'.
2
Find the middle term
The middle term is 'able to bring the sun from the west' (القادر على الإتيان بالشمس من المغرب). It appears as predicate in both premises: in the first it is negated, in the second it is affirmed.
In the second figure, the middle term is the predicate in both premises.
3
Check the quantity and quality of premises
For a valid second-figure syllogism, one premise must be negative and the major premise must be universal. Here the minor premise ('you are not able') is universal negative (implied), and the major premise ('my lord is able') is universal affirmative. Thus the conclusion is a universal negative: 'You are not my lord.'
If either premise were particular, the syllogism might be invalid.
4
Compare with the first figure
Unlike the first figure, the second figure does not yield a direct positive conclusion; it always yields a negative conclusion. Here it proves that Nimrod is not a lord.
The author notes that the argument can be expressed in multiple figures, showing the richness of the Quranic text.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Ibrahim's Argument in Multiple Syllogistic Forms
Author analyzes the syllogism from Quran 6:76 and expresses it in second, first, exceptive, and fourth figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibrahim's argument from Quran 6:76 in at least three different syllogistic figures and the exceptive form.

Core Idea
One logical argument can be validly expressed in multiple syllogistic forms.

In this passage, the author takes the famous Quranic argument of Prophet Ibrahim from the setting of celestial bodies and shows how it can be cast in four different syllogistic forms. This demonstrates the flexibility and universality of logical reasoning embodied in the Islamic theological tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core premises
The argument is: 'This celestial body sets; whatever sets is not a lord; therefore this is not a lord.' Note the major (universal negative) and minor (singular affirmative).
2
Second figure formulation
The author arranges the syllogism with the middle term 'setting thing' as predicate in both premises. The major is 'No setting thing is a lord' and the minor 'This is a setting thing', yielding 'This is not a lord'.
In the second figure, the middle term is predicate of both premises.
3
First figure formulation
The author notes this is easier. Rearrange so that the middle term is subject of the major and predicate of the minor: 'This is a setting thing; no setting thing is a lord; therefore this is not a lord.' This is the natural first figure.
The first figure is the most intuitive and directly yields the conclusion.
4
Exceptive (conditional) formulation
The argument is recast as a conditional syllogism: 'If this were my lord, it would not set; but it set; therefore it is not my lord.' This is a classic modus tollens.
The exceptive syllogism uses a conditional premise and an exception.
5
Fourth figure formulation
The author shows a less common form where the middle term is predicate of the minor and subject of the major: 'The setting thing is not a lord; this is a setting thing; therefore my lord is not this,' which converts to 'This is not my lord.'
The fourth figure requires conversion to obtain the direct conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشكل الثاني : ففي استدلال إبراهيم الخليل أيضًا ـ عليه وعلى نبينا الصلاة والسلام - بالأفول على نفي الربوبية عن الكواكب والقمر والشمس في قوله - تعالى -: ﴿ فَلَمَّا جَنَّ عَلَيْهِ أَلَيْلُ رَوَا كَوْكَبَا الآية [الأنعام ٧٦] ؛ لأن قوله : ﴿ فَلَمَّا أَفَلَ قَالَ لَا أُحِبُّ الْأَفِلِينَ ) في قوة قوله : هذه آفلة ليس بآفل، ينتج من الشكل الثاني : هذه لست وربي بربي، فالصغرى من قوله فلما أفل، والكبرى من قوله : (لا أحب الآفلين). إذ المعنى لا أحب عبادتهم ؛ لأن الرب وهو الذي يستحق أن يعبد وحده لا يأفل أبدًا. ويمكن سوقه من الشكل الأول، وهو أسهل، بأن يقال : هذه آفلة، ولا شيء من الآفل بربي، ينتج : هذه ليست بربي . ويمكن سوقه من الاستثنائي بأن يقال: لو كانت هذه ربي لما أفلت، لكنها أفلت ينتج : فليست بربي . ويمكن سوقه من الشكل الرابع بأن يقال : الآفل ليس بربي، وهذه آفلة، ينتج : ربي ليس بهذه وينعكس إلى هذه ليست بربي، لكن :
And as for the second figure: It is in the argument of Ibrahim, the Friend — peace be upon him and upon our Prophet — by means of setting to negate lordship from the planets, the moon, and the sun in His saying — Exalted is He: 'When the night covered him, he saw a star...' the verse [Al-An'am 6:76]. Because his saying: 'When it set, he said: I do not love those that set' is equivalent to his saying: 'This is a setting thing, not a lord,' so it yields from the second figure: 'This is not my lord.' So the minor premise is from his saying 'when it set,' and the major premise from his saying 'I do not love those that set' — meaning I do not love their worship, because the lord, who deserves to be worshipped alone, never sets. And it can be conducted from the first figure, which is easier, by saying: 'This is a setting thing, and no setting thing is a lord,' so it yields: 'This is not my lord.' And it can be conducted from the exceptive by saying: 'If this were my lord, it would not set, but it set,' so it yields: 'It is not my lord.' And it can be conducted from the fourth figure by saying: 'The setting thing is not a lord, and this is a setting thing,' so it yields: 'My lord is not this,' which converts to: 'This is not my lord.' But:
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage expands on the second major Quranic example of Ibrahim's argument using the setting of celestial bodies (Surah Al-An'am 6:76-78). The author shows how this argument can be expressed in four different syllogistic forms. The basic argument is: The celestial bodies (star, moon, sun) set, and whatever sets cannot be a lord, therefore they are not lords. The author first presents it as a second-figure syllogism: the minor premise 'this sets' (from the narrative of seeing the star setting), and the major premise 'whatever sets is not a lord' (derived from Ibrahim's statement 'I do not love those that set,' understood as 'I do not worship them because a true lord never sets'). The conclusion is 'this is not my lord.' Next, he shows the first-figure formulation: 'This is a setting thing; no setting thing is a lord; therefore this is not a lord.' The author notes this first-figure form is easier (as it is the most natural). Then he presents the exceptive (conditional) form: 'If this were my lord, it would not set; but it set; therefore it is not my lord.' Finally, he presents a fourth-figure formulation: 'The setting thing is not a lord; this is a setting thing; therefore my lord is not this,' which converts to 'this is not my lord.' The text ends abruptly with 'But:' possibly indicating a criticism or further refinement, but the remainder is cut off. Overall, the passage demonstrates that the same Quranic argument can be validly cast in multiple syllogistic moods, showing the universality of logical form.

Checkpoint
In the analysis of Ibrahim's argument from setting (al-ufool), which syllogistic form does the author describe as 'easier' (أسهل)?
A) The second figure.
B) The first figure.
C) The exceptive syllogism.
D) The fourth figure.

Key Takeaway
The same divine argument can be expressed in multiple syllogistic forms, each valid, demonstrating the richness of Islamic logic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the following argument from Hadith: 'Every intoxicant is haram; khamr is an intoxicant; therefore khamr is haram.' Express this in the second figure and in the exceptive form.
?
Identify which figure Ibrahim's argument would be in if the major premise were 'The lord never sets' and the minor were 'The star sets'.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Reformulating the Sun Argument Across Figures
Author presents a proof about the sun and shows its formulations in first and second figures

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reformulate a categorical syllogism from the first figure to the second figure and identify the middle term.

Core Idea
A single valid argument can be expressed in multiple syllogistic figures.

The author presents a theological proof — if you cannot bring the sun from the west while the true Lord can, then you are not the Lord. This proof can be structured as a categorical syllogism. The passage shows how the same logical content can be arranged in the first figure and then in the second figure, with the middle term shifting position. Understanding this flexibility is crucial for analyzing arguments in Islamic theological debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion
The conclusion of the argument is 'You are not my Lord' (فلست بربي). This is the claim being proven.
2
Extract the premises
The premises are: (a) You are not able to bring the sun from the west; (b) My Lord is able to bring the sun from the west. These are explicitly given for the second figure formulation.
In the first figure, the premises might be: 'Whoever cannot bring the sun is not the Lord; you cannot bring it.'
3
Determine the middle term
The middle term (الحد الوسط) is 'ability to bring the sun' (القدرة على الإتيان بالشمس). It appears in both premises but not in the conclusion.
4
Compare the figures
In the first figure, the middle term is subject in the major premise and predicate in the minor premise. In the second figure, the middle term is predicate in both premises. Write out both forms to see the difference.
First figure: All who cannot bring the sun are not the Lord (major); You cannot bring the sun (minor); therefore You are not the Lord (conclusion). Second figure: My Lord can bring the sun (major); You cannot bring the sun (minor); conclusion same.
5
Recognize the flexibility
The author notes that the verse is not restricted to the first figure; both formulations are valid. The first figure is considered more apparent, but the second is also correct.
This teaches that a proof can be recast in multiple figures without losing validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لقدرت على الإتيان بالشمس من المغرب لكنك لا تقدر عليه، ينتج : فلست بربي . ويمكن أن يساق من الشكل الثاني بأن يقال : ما أنت قادر على أن تأتي بالشمس من المغرب وربي قادر على أن يأتي بها، ينتج : فلست بربي؛ فليس المقصود حصر المستنبط من الآية في الشكل الأول، وكأنهم رأوه أظهر من غيره .
You are able to bring the sun from the west, but you are not able to do it, therefore: You are not my Lord. And it can be drawn in the second figure by saying: You are not able to bring the sun from the west, and my Lord is able to bring it, therefore: You are not my Lord. So the intended meaning is not to restrict what is deduced from the verse to the first figure, but they saw it as more apparent than others.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage shows how a single logical proof can be formulated in multiple syllogistic figures. The proof is an argument against someone claiming divinity: if you cannot bring the sun from the west, while the true Lord can, then you are not the Lord. The author first gives the straightforward first figure form (though not explicitly stated as such here; it is implied by the direct contrast of abilities). Then he recasts it in the second figure, where the middle term 'ability to bring the sun' becomes the predicate in both premises. He notes that the verse is not confined to the first figure, though the first figure is considered more evident. This illustrates a key principle in Islamic logic: a valid argument can be expressed in multiple figures, and the choice of figure depends on clarity and naturalness.

Key Terms
الشكل الأول
الشكل الثاني
الحد الوسط

Checkpoint
In the sun argument, what is the middle term in the second figure formulation?
A) Bringing the sun from the west
B) Ability to bring the sun
C) Being a Lord
D) The sun itself

Key Takeaway
The same logical content can be validly expressed in different syllogistic figures; understanding figure flexibility is essential in analyzing arguments in Islamic theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the standard Kalam proof: 'The world is temporally originated; everything temporally originated has a cause; therefore the world has a cause.' Reformulate this argument in the second figure, identifying the new middle term.
?
In the sun argument, if we use 'Lord of the universe' as the middle term, which figure would that produce? Explain your reasoning.
